A. 
Project Development Objective 
1. 
Project 
development 
objective: 
(see 
Annex 
1) 
households 
and 
groups 
in 
about 840 
villages 
in 
a 
sustainable 
manner 
through 
the 
implementation 
of 
small 
agricultural 
development 
subprojects 
planned and 
managed 
by 
community 
members 
and 
farmers' 
groups. 
This 
objective 
will 
be 
achieved 
by: 
(i) 
empowering 
self-selected 
rural 
communities 
and farmers' groups 
to 
make decisions 
regarding 
choice 
of 
sustainable 
and remunerative 
productive 
technology; 
(ii) 
sharing 
of 
costs 
by 
the 
public 
sector 
and 
participants, 
and 
hence 
sharing 
the 
risk 
of 
adoption 
of 
improved 
technologies, 
again 
for 
self-selected participants; 
(iii) 
enhancing 
demand 
for 
products 
and 
services 
provided 
by 
the 
private 
sector 
in 
rural 
areas 
by 
increasing 
the 
purchasing 
power 
of 
participating 
groups 
and 
encouraging 
the 
growth 
of 
savings; 
(iv) promoting 
improved land 
and 
crop 
husbandry practices 
by 
participants; 
(v) 
supporting 
the 
ongoing decentralization 
process 
at 
the 
district 
level; 
and 
(vi) 
partially 
financing 
maintenance 
and/or 
construction 
of 
roads, 
bridges, 
and 
other 
small 
subprojects 
to improve 
access 
to 
markets. 
The 
project 
seeks 
to 
raise 
the 
production 
of 
food, 
incomes, 
and 
assets 
of 
participating 
2. 
Key 
performance 
indicators: 
(see 
Annex 
1) 
districts 
and villages: 
(i) 
increases 
in 
rural 
incomes, 
assets 
and 
liquidity 
of 
participating 
farmers, 
compared 
to 
control 
groups 
and pre-project 
levels; 
(ii) 
increases 
in 
agricultural 
productivity 
of 
participating 
farms; 
(iii) 
improvement 
in 
management 
and 
maintenance 
of 
agricultural 
and 
rural 
infrastructure financed 
by 
the 
project; 
and 
(iv) 
volume 
of 
private 
trade 
in 
agricultural 
inputs, 
marketed 
output and 
services 
provision 
in 
participating 
communities and districts. 
Performance 
of 
the 
project 
will 
be 
monitored through 
the 
following 
indicators 
in 
participating 
B. 
Strategic Context 
1. 
Sector-related 
Country 
Assistance 
Strategy 
(CAS) 
goal supported 
by 
the project: 
(see 
Annex 
1) 
Document number: 
20426 
Date 
of 
latest 
CAS 
discussion: 
06/15/00 
Tanzania 
has 
undertaken ambitious 
reforms 
and 
the 
economy 
is 
growing 
and 
responding 
positively. 
Smallholders 
in 
the 
agricultural 
sector, 
however, have 
not 
been 
able 
to 
benefit 
appreciably 
from 
the 
improved 
economic environment. 
The 
smallholder 
agricultural 
sector 
is 
a 
large 
share 
of 
the 
overall 
economy, 
and 
poor 
performance constrains 
aggregate 
growth 
and 
impedes 
reduction 
of 
poverty. 
The poor 
performance, 
in 
turn, 
derives 
in 
large 
part 
from 
inability 
of 
smallholders 
to 
adopt technologies 
that 
are 
available, 
known 
to 
the scientific community, and 
are 
sound 
from both 
economic and 
environmental 
perspectives. 
Adoption 
of 
technology 
is 
constrained 
by 
several factors. 
Many 
smallholders 
are 
not 
fully 
aware 
of 
the 
options 
available 
to 
them. 
When 
aware, 
they 
may 
be 
unable 
to 
purchase 
even 
the 
modest array 
of 
inputs 
required 
to 
adopt 
the 
technologies. 
When 
neither 
cash 
nor 
knowledge 
constrains choice, 
some 
producers 
are 
unwilling 
to 
assume 
the 
full 
risk 
of 
adopting 
new 
approaches. 
This 
project 
seeks 
to provide improved 
opportunities 
to 
smallholders 
to 
make better 
use 
of 
existing 
technologies 
and 
to 
adopt 
improved 
ones. 
The 
project 
is 
fully 
consistent 
with 
the strategy 
outlined 
in 
the 
CAS 
and 
PRSP progress 
report 
discussed 
at 
the 
Board 
November 
27,2001 (IDA/SecM2001-0643), 
which 
recognizes that 
poverty 
in 
Tanzania 
is 
largely 
rural, and 
that 
lack 
of 
opportunities 
for 
poor 
people 
to improve 
their 
incomes 
is 
at 
the 
root 
of 
problem. 
The 
rural 
poor 
earn 
their 
livelihoods 
primarily from 
agriculture. 
The 
CAS 
also 
recognizes 
protection 
of 
biodiversity 
and forests 
as 
important 
objectives, 
and 
these 
can 
be 
addressed 
by 
enhancing 
the 
ability 
of 
smallholders 
to 
make 
more 
sustainable 
use 
of 
the 
land 
presently 
under 
cultivation 
and 
to 
minimize 
risks 
of 
crop failure, 
thereby 
reducing 
expansion 
of 
production 
onto 
more 
-2- 
Page  9
fragile 
lands 
or 
inappropriate 
exploitation 
of 
forests 
to 
generate 
income. 
The 
CAS 
specifically 
recommends an 
IDA-supported 
operation 
to improve agricultural 
productivity for 
smallholders. 
This 
project 
is 
prepared 
to 
respond 
to 
that 
mandate. 
2. 
Main 
sector 
issues 
and 
Government 
strategy: 
approximately 
80% 
of 
the 
country’s labor 
force. 
Approximately 
3.5 
million 
farm 
families cultivate 
about 
4.5 
million 
hectares 
of 
land. 
Most 
of 
these 
people 
are 
extremely 
poor 
and 
use 
low 
inputllow 
output 
agricultural 
systems 
that 
lead 
over 
time to 
depletion 
of 
soil 
nutrients. 
Yields 
of 
the 
country’s 
principal 
food 
crops 
are 
only 
20% 
to 
40% 
of 
the 
potential, 
and 
are 
currently 
stagnant 
or 
declining. 
Low 
productivity 
leads 
to 
a 
vicious circle 
of 
poverty, 
resource degradation, 
and 
food 
insecurity. 
Agriculture 
contributes 
about 50% 
of 
Tanzania’s 
GDP, 
75% 
of 
export 
earnings, 
and 
employs 
Five 
key 
issues 
are 
relevant 
to 
the 
project’s objectives 
and 
design: 
(i) 
Reforms 
over 
the 
last 
decade 
have created 
a 
good 
foundation 
for 
agricultural 
growth, 
but 
several 
factors 
have 
delayed a 
significant 
positive 
supply 
response 
and 
have 
increased 
risks. 
Removal 
of 
subsidies 
for 
agricultural 
inputs 
increased 
the 
costs 
and 
risks 
of 
higher 
yielding 
technologies 
in 
many 
parts 
of 
the 
country. 
As 
demand 
for 
inputs 
fell, 
retailers and 
traders 
exited 
from 
local 
input 
markets and 
competition 
declined. 
Low 
volumes 
of 
trade 
and 
limited 
competition 
raised 
trading 
margins 
and 
reduced 
the 
profitability 
of 
purchased 
inputs 
to 
smallholder 
producers. 
The 
recent 
real 
appreciation 
of 
the 
Tanzanian 
Shilling 
has 
made 
export 
crops 
less 
competitive, 
and 
has 
highlighted 
the 
need 
to 
increase 
productivity. 
In 
order 
to avoid 
deepening 
poverty, smallholder 
farmers 
must 
adopt 
improved 
technologies that 
yield 
higher 
returns 
to 
all 
factors 
of 
production 
while 
at 
the 
same 
time 
preserving 
the 
quality 
of 
their 
main 
non-labor 
asset, 
land. 
The 
volume 
of 
activity 
and performance 
of 
rural 
markets 
must 
also 
improve, 
so 
that 
incremental output 
can 
be 
placed 
at 
profitable 
margins, 
and 
inputs 
purchased 
at 
competitive 
prices. 
(ii) 
Governmental 
officials 
are 
increasingly 
aware 
of 
the 
importance 
of 
farmers‘ 
participation 
in 
decisions 
regarding allocations 
of 
scarce 
funds 
for 
public 
investments. 
The 
growing 
number 
of 
successful experiences 
in 
Participatory 
approaches 
in 
Tanzania complement 
to 
the 
ongoing 
process 
of 
decentralization 
to 
the 
District 
level. 
Agricultural 
Sector 
Development Program 
(ASDP) 
aims 
at 
channelling approximately 
75% 
of 
all 
public 
investments 
in 
agriculture 
through 
district 
councils. 
This 
would 
facilitate 
the 
work 
of 
district 
extension 
staff 
and 
the 
delivery 
of 
other 
support services 
to 
develop small, 
medium 
and 
large 
scale 
production 
and 
related 
enterprises. 
(iii) 
Degradation 
of 
natural 
resources, 
particularly 
land, 
is 
increasing 
in 
most 
regions 
of 
the 
country. 
Continuous cropping, 
non-replenishment 
of 
nutrients 
extracted 
from 
the soil, 
erosion 
by 
wind 
and 
water, 
and 
poor land 
husbandry practices 
contribute 
to 
depletion 
of 
the 
native 
fertility 
of 
soils 
and 
depress 
yields. 
The 
long 
term 
sustainability 
of 
agriculture 
on 
these 
lands 
is 
at 
serious 
risk. 
Farmers 
throughout 
the 
country complain 
of 
“soil 
exhaustion” 
as 
one 
of 
the 
most 
important 
problems 
they 
face. 
Deforestation 
in 
catchment 
areas has 
led 
to 
loss 
of 
ground 
cover, 
causing 
soil 
moisture 
losses 
and 
persistent drought. 
The 
need 
for 
better 
management 
of 
soil 
and water 
resources 
is 
therefore paramount. 
(iv) 
Smallholders seeking 
to improve 
their 
productivity 
and 
incomes 
need 
access 
to 
good 
advisory 
services. 
The 
recent 
decentralization 
of 
the 
delivery 
of 
rural 
services, 
including 
agricultural 
extension, 
to district 
councils 
has 
launched 
a 
promising 
process 
of 
reform 
in 
dissemination 
of 
-3- 
Page  10
technology. 
Pluralism 
in 
provision 
of 
extension 
services, 
including 
the 
use 
of 
private 
service 
providers 
and 
NGOs, 
is 
slowly 
emerging. 
In 
the 
interim, 
however, 
neither 
research 
nor 
extension 
is 
capable 
without 
additional 
measures 
to provide 
the 
needed 
level 
of 
support 
to 
farmers. 
(v) 
The 
withdrawal 
of 
the 
state 
and parastatal 
entities 
from 
the 
provision 
of 
inputs, 
marketing 
of 
output, 
and 
supply 
of 
credit 
opens 
opportunities 
for 
a 
much 
expanded 
role for 
the 
private 
sector. 
Yet 
private 
agents 
in 
input 
supply 
and 
rural 
finance have 
found 
the 
terrain 
risky 
and 
have 
been 
very 
cautious 
in 
entry, 
in 
part 
because 
markets 
are 
thin, 
clients 
have 
little 
collateral, and 
costs 
of 
information 
are 
high. 
As 
a 
consequence, 
most smallholders have 
poor 
access 
to 
marketing 
and 
financial 
services 
needed 
for 
adoption 
of 
higher 
yielding 
technology. 
Government 
strategy: 
Government 
seeks 
to 
address 
these 
problems through an integrated and 
participatory 
approach 
that 
brings 
together 
all 
stakeholders 
in 
identifying 
the 
key 
problems, 
prioritizing 
the 
needs, 
and 
developing the 
strategies 
and 
technical 
solutions 
to 
address 
the 
challenges. 
The 
Participatory 
Agricultural 
Development and Empowerment Project (PADEP) 
is 
an 
integral part 
of 
the 
Agricultural 
Sector 
Development 
Program 
(ASDP)  and 
the 
broader 
Rural 
Development 
Strategy 
(RDS). 
Government 
has 
completed 
the 
ASDP 
Framework 
and 
process 
Document 
which 
focuses 
on 
(i) 
empowering 
of 
rural 
communities 
and farmer 
groups, 
(ii) 
increasing agricultural 
productivity 
and 
access 
to 
markets, and 
(iii) 
decentralizing 
public 
investments and 
involving 
the 
private 
sector 
in 
service 
delivery. 
PADEP 
will 
facilitate 
implementation 
of 
the 
ASDP 
along 
the 
agreed 
core 
principles. 
The 
overall 
objective 
of 
the 
Rural 
Development 
Strategy 
is 
to 
reduce 
poverty through 
multi-sectoral interventions 
in 
agriculture, 
roads, water, education, 
health 
and 
local 
government 
reforms. 
The 
Strategy 
will 
harmonize and 
coordinate 
key 
sector 
policies 
and 
strategies. 
The 
Rural 
Development 
Strategy 
will 
be 
implemented 
within 
the 
framework 
of 
decentralization 
and 
will 
rely 
on 
operational 
mechanisms 
that empower 
rural 
communities 
in 
planning 
and 
implementing 
development projects. 
The 
PADEP 
project 
will 
build 
on 
the 
“Vision 
for 
Extension 
for 
2010,” 
the 
articulation 
of 
the 
Government’s 
approach 
to 
extension 
and 
the 
“Medium 
Term 
Plan“ 
for 
the 
National Agricultural 
Research System 
(NARS), 
which 
highlights 
the 
need 
for 
generation and 
adoption 
of 
technology 
within 
the 
ASDP 
framework. 
To 
achieve the 
Agricultural 
Sector 
Development 
Strategy 
(ASDS) objective 
of 
5 
percent annual 
agricultural 
growth, 
the 
Government 
intends 
to 
maintain 
macro-economic 
stability 
and 
ensure 
that 
macro-economic 
policies (both fiscal 
and monetary) 
promote 
efficiency 
and 
competitiveness 
of 
the 
agricultural 
sector; create 
an enabling environment 
for 
improving 
profitability 
of 
the 
sector; increase 
smallholder 
productivity 
(through 
promotion 
of 
adoption 
of 
improved 
technologies); 
facilitate 
increased 
availability 
of 
key 
inputs 
(especially 
improved 
seeds 
and 
fertilizers); 
assist 
smallholders 
to 
improve 
their 
access 
to 
credit 
(through 
strengthening 
rural 
financial 
institutions); 
maintain 
competitiveness 
in 
agricultural 
exports 
(by 
removing 
external 
trade 
barriers);  enhance 
the 
efficiency 
and 
sustainability 
of 
land utilization 
(through 
promotion 
of 
better 
land 
husbandry 
practices); 
and 
improve 
incentives 
for 
the 
private 
sector 
(through 
fiscal 
policy 
and 
better 
business 
environment). 
The 
Government’s 
role 
will 
be 
limited 
to 
policy 
formulation, 
establishment 
of 
a 
regulatory 
framework 
to 
reduce 
transaction 
costs, 
provision 
of 
strategic 
public 
goods, 
and 
maintenance 
of 
safety 
nets 
for 
the 
most vulnerable 
in 
the 
society. 
Further, 
the 
Government 
intends 
to 
ensure 
that 
it 
does 
not 
undertake 
delivery 
of 
goods 
and 
services 
that 
the 
private 
sector 
is 
able 
to provide 
efficiently 
and 
effectively. 
-4- 
Page  11
3. 
Sector 
issues 
to 
be 
addressed 
by 
the 
project 
and 
strategic 
choices: 
Dialogue 
on 
key 
issues 
related 
to 
agricultural 
policy 
is 
undertaken 
by 
stakeholders 
and 
development 
partners 
jointly 
within 
the 
context 
of 
the 
Agricultural 
Sector 
Development 
Program, and 
further 
supported 
by 
the 
matrix 
of 
actions 
identified 
in 
the 
sequence 
of 
Poverty Reduction 
Strategy 
Credits 
included 
in 
the 
Country 
Assistance Strategy. 
For 
that 
reason, 
the 
PADEP 
project 
does 
not 
specifically 
aim to 
accomplish 
policy 
changes. 
Identification 
of 
key 
issues 
that constrain 
agricultural 
growth 
has 
been part 
of 
the 
process 
of 
preparation 
of 
the 
project, 
however, and 
the 
issues 
are 
enumerated 
below. 
Among 
these 
are 
the 
following: 
1. 
Grain 
marketing 
policy: 
i.e., 
reaffirmation 
and enforcement 
of 
unencumbered 
internal 
and 
external 
trade 
in 
agricultural 
commodities, 
improved marketing information, revision 
of 
operational 
procedures 
governing 
the 
Strategic 
Grain 
Reserve; 
2. 
Rationalization 
of 
local 
levies and 
taxation 
and enforcement 
of 
the 
decisions 
taken; 
3. 
Policies regarding fertilizer, 
seeds, 
and 
plant protection 
agencies, 
e.g. 
fertilizer 
policy 
- 
efforts to 
facilitate 
bulk 
importation 
of 
fertilizers and 
revision 
of 
management 
of 
KR-I1 
fertilizer 
imports, 
seed 
policy 
- 
adoption 
of 
quality control 
of 
seeds, 
and 
plant protection 
- 
investment 
in 
additional 
work 
on 
integrated 
pest management; 
and 
4. 
Disposition 
of 
commercial 
state-owned 
agricultural 
assets 
to 
create 
opportunities 
for 
the 
private 
sector 
and 
to 
provide 
reassurance 
that 
the 
state 
will 
not 
remain 
active 
as 
a 
competitor 
with 
special 
advantage, 
e.g. 
state 
farms, 
agro-industries and 
warehouses. 
The 
steps 
that the Government 
is 
taking 
with 
regard 
to 
each 
of 
the 
above 
are 
outlined 
in 
the 
Letter 
of 
Sectoral 
Policy. 
In 
brief 
they 
are 
as 
follows: 
1. 
The 
Government 
has 
continued 
to 
implement 
the 
grain 
market 
liberalization 
policy 
that started 
in 
the 
mid-l980’s, 
including 
removal 
of 
the 
remaining 
trade 
barriers and 
enforcement 
of 
free 
internal 
and 
external 
trade 
in 
agricultural 
commodities, 
improved marketing 
information 
and 
revision 
of 
operational 
procedures 
governing 
the 
Strategic 
Grain 
Reserve (SGR). 
So 
far, the 
Government 
has 
liberalized 
trade 
in 
grain 
with 
regional 
(EAC 
and 
SADC) 
partners. 
In 
the 
past 
two 
years, 
private 
traders 
in 
the 
country 
exported maize 
to 
the 
Eastern, 
Central 
and Southern 
African 
countries (Kenya, 
Malawi, 
Zambia, 
Democratic 
Republic 
of 
Congo 
and Zimbabwe), 
which 
were 
hit 
by 
severe 
drought. 
Significant 
amounts 
of 
maize 
were 
also 
sold to 
the 
World 
Food 
Program 
(WFP) 
and 
UNHCR, 
and 
distributed 
to 
refugees’ 
camps 
within 
the country. 
The 
Government 
has 
removed 
all 
road 
blocks 
to 
encourage 
free domestic trade 
in 
agricultural 
commodities, 
particularly 
staple 
foodstuffs. 
The 
Strategic 
Grain 
Reserve (SGR) 
has 
been 
separated 
from 
the 
Food 
Security 
department. 
The 
latter 
has 
now 
become a 
fully-fledged 
directorate 
of 
MAFS 
dealing 
with 
food 
policy 
issues. 
The 
current 
role 
of 
SGR 
is 
to 
purchase 
maize 
at 
market 
prices, 
up 
to 
a 
maximum holding 
of 
150,000 
tons, 
and 
to 
release 
the 
stocks 
during 
food 
shortages 
or natural 
disasters 
at 
prevailing 
market 
prices. 
Under 
the 
Government 
of 
Ireland 
support, 
the 
Government 
is 
currently 
preparing 
a 
National 
Food 
Security 
Policy.  Similarly, 
the 
IFAD 
funded 
Agricultural Marketing 
Systems 
Development 
Project 
(AMSDP) 
is 
supporting 
the 
development 
of 
the 
Agricultural Marketing 
Policy. 
These 
policy 
documents 
will 
provide framework 
for 
completing 
the 
outstanding 
agricultural 
sector 
policy 
reforms. 
2. 
The 
Government 
has 
recognized 
that 
proliferation 
of 
local 
government 
taxes 
and levies 
have 
impeded 
some 
economic 
activities 
and 
is 
undertaking 
efforts 
to 
rationalize 
them. 
An 
upper 
limit 
of 
5 
percent 
has 
been imposed 
on 
the 
crop 
cess 
charged 
by 
district 
councils. 
This 
directive 
has 
been 
included 
in 
-5- 
Page  12
the 
Finance 
Act 
of 
2002 
and 
is 
being 
enforced through 
the 
President's 
Office, 
Regional 
Administration 
and 
Local 
Government, 
which 
is 
responsible 
for 
the 
administration 
of 
local 
authorities. 
In 
addition, 
guidelines 
for 
rationalization 
and 
harmonization 
of 
Local 
Government 
revenue 
sources 
were 
issued 
to 
local 
authorities 
in 
December, 
2002 
to 
assist 
them 
in 
reforming 
their 
taxation 
systems. 
Attention 
was 
given 
to 
reducing 
the 
number 
of 
taxes, 
particularly 
'nuisance taxes' 
as 
on 
the 
average, 
local 
authorities have 
about 
40 
different 
taxes, 
with 
10 
percent 
of 
these 
accounting 
for 
about 
70 
percent 
of 
total 
revenue. 
To 
ensure 
that 
minimum 
standards 
for 
service 
delivery 
are 
maintained, 
with 
the 
likely 
lower 
level 
of 
local 
revenue, 
the 
central 
government 
has 
commissioned a 
study 
on 
revenue 
sharing 
mechanisms. 
At 
national 
level, 
duties 
on 
packaging materials 
for 
agricultural 
inputs 
have been removed 
in 
order 
to 
encourage 
bulk 
importation 
of 
inputs 
and 
re-bagging 
in 
the 
country, 
hence 
reducing 
the 
cost 
of 
inputs 
to 
farmers. 
Under 
the 
Business 
Environment 
Strengthening 
(BEST) 
program, 
to 
be 
implemented 
from 
July, 
2003, 
the 
Government 
will 
continue 
to 
focus 
attention 
on 
harmonization 
of 
local 
government 
taxation 
to 
reduce 
the 
tax 
burden 
on 
private 
enterprises 
and 
review 
exporthmport 
procedures 
to 
reduce 
costs 
related 
to 
time 
required 
to 
meet 
procedures, 
and 
to 
reduce 
opportunities 
for 
corruption. 
This 
will 
reduce 
transactions 
costs 
in 
marketing 
and attract 
further 
agricultural 
investments. 
3. 
The 
Government 
with 
assistance 
from 
JICA 
is 
reviewing 
procedures 
for 
the 
importation 
and 
distribution 
of 
KR-I1 
fertilizer. 
In 
terms 
of 
seed 
policy, 
the 
Parliament recently 
passed 
Plant 
Breeder's 
Right 
Act 
(2002), 
which 
protects 
the 
intellectual 
property 
right 
of 
the 
local 
breeders. 
It 
also 
gives 
the 
responsibility 
of 
multiplying 
breeder 
seeds 
to 
the 
agricultural 
research 
institutions 
in 
order 
to 
maintain 
purity. 
The 
Seed 
Act 
(1973), 
provides 
for 
the 
Government 
to 
establish a 
National 
Seed 
Executive 
Agency, 
which 
will 
be 
responsible 
for 
seed 
certification, 
screening 
and 
inspection. 
Further, the Government 
through 
its 
Plant Protection 
Act 
(1997) 
and 
associated 
regulations 
is 
promoting 
Integrated 
Pest 
Management 
(IPM) 
practices 
to 
minimize 
the 
use 
of 
pesticides, 
and 
reduce 
the 
risks 
of 
environmental 
degradation. 
Under 
regional integration initiatives, 
the 
East 
African 
Governments 
(Tanzania, 
Kenya 
and 
Uganda) have 
signed 
a 
protocol to 
harmonize 
Seed 
Regulatory 
Policies 
and 
Legislations. 
The 
Cabinet 
has 
recently 
approved 
the 
Institutional 
and 
Legal 
Framework 
for 
Environmental 
Protection, 
which 
gives 
the 
role 
of 
policy 
guidance 
to 
the 
Vice 
President's 
Department 
of 
Environment, 
overall 
regulatory 
and enforcement 
to 
the 
National 
Environmental 
Management 
Council 
and 
sectoral 
mandates 
of 
environmental 
regulation 
to 
line 
ministries. 
The 
process 
of 
drafting 
an environmental 
law 
is 
about 
to 
start. 
4. 
The 
Government 
has 
already 
taken 
an 
inventory 
and 
evaluation 
of 
state-owned 
productive 
assets 
in 
the 
agricultural 
sector. 
The 
Government 
has 
rehabilitated 
and 
leased 
two 
cashew 
factories (Masasi 
and Kibaha) 
to 
local 
private 
investors, 
with 
a 
view to 
encouraging domestic processing and 
adding 
value. 
The 
Government 
has 
also 
completed 
a 
study 
of 
warehousing 
and 
has 
decided 
to 
dispose 
of 
most 
of 
them 
through 
outright 
sale 
and 
earmarked a 
few 
other 
strategically located 
for 
leasing. 
Regarding 
rice 
state 
farms 
(Kapunga, 
Rum, 
Mbarali 
and 
Dakawa), the 
Government 
has 
divested 
by 
redistributing 
land 
to 
the 
local 
smallholder 
farmers. 
A 
decision 
has 
also 
been 
made 
to 
transfer 
the 
relatively 
larger wheat (Hanang 
and 
West 
Kilimanjaro) 
and 
maize 
(Namtumbo) 
state 
farms 
to 
the 
Land 
Bank. 
These 
will 
be 
leased 
to 
large commercial 
farming 
firms 
by 
the 
Tanzania Investment 
Center (TIC). 
In 
terms 
of 
financing 
of 
inputs, 
the Government's 
Agricultural 
Input 
Fund 
(for 
importing 
fertilizer, 
seed 
and 
agro-chemicals) 
is 
now 
managed 
by 
commercial 
banks 
(Exim 
and 
CRDB 
banks). 
Loans 
are 
issued 
on 
commercial 
terms, 
and 
its 
scope 
has 
been 
expanded 
to 
cover 
agricultural 
machinery 
and 
equipment. 
-6- 
Page  13
Strategic choices: 
The 
project 
design 
is 
based 
on 
the 
following 
key 
principles: 
The 
project 
will 
be 
implemented largely 
at 
the 
levels 
of 
districts 
and 
rural 
communities. 
About 
80% 
of 
the 
project 
funds 
will 
be 
invested 
in 
subprojects 
of 
rural 
community 
and 
farmer 
groups, 
and 
in 
financing 
capacity 
building 
at 
the 
local 
level. 
At 
the 
national 
level, 
the 
project 
will 
provide 
modest support 
for 
further 
analytical 
work to 
support 
policy 
and regulatory reforms 
to 
enhance 
the 
participation 
of 
the 
private 
sector 
in 
inputloutput 
markets and 
provision 
of 
services, 
and 
for 
the 
coordination 
unit. 
There 
will 
be 
no 
direct 
budgetary 
support 
to 
line 
ministries. 
The project 
will 
be 
implemented 
in 
a 
phased 
manner 
starting 
in 
selected 
districts 
(including 
in 
Zanzibar) 
according 
to 
demonstrated 
implementation capacity and 
the 
existence 
of 
viable 
farmers’ 
groups. 
The project 
implementation 
arrangements 
are 
flexible 
to 
allow 
for 
faster 
implementation 
if 
the 
absorptive 
capacity 
is 
enhanced, 
either 
by 
an 
increased 
number 
of 
participating 
districts 
or 
by 
the 
incorporation 
of 
more 
villages 
within 
districts, 
or more 
farmer 
groups 
within 
villages. 
The 
project 
will 
not 
finance, 
make special 
foreign 
exchange 
provisions, 
or 
establish 
credit 
lines 
for 
the 
importation 
of 
fertilizers or 
any 
other 
agricultural 
input. 
The 
project 
will 
not 
provide 
any 
lines 
of 
credit, 
but 
will 
support 
promotion 
of 
rural 
savings 
and credit 
by 
strengthening 
farmer 
groups 
and, 
under 
certain applications 
of 
the 
project 
guidelines, 
requiring 
participants 
to 
have 
active 
savings accounts. 
The project 
will 
support 
gender 
equity 
and 
HIVIAIDS 
awareness 
and 
mitigation, 
as 
part 
of 
the 
community participatory planning 
activities. 
The project 
will 
focus 
on 
increased 
agricultural production 
from 
areas 
already 
under 
cultivation, 
as 
this 
will 
cause 
minimal 
ecological 
disturbance, 
will 
not involve 
costly 
movement 
of 
population 
or 
heavy investments 
in 
new 
areas, 
and can 
be 
accomplished 
faster 
and 
with 
benign 
environmental 
consequences. 
C. 
Project Description 
Summary 
1. 
Project 
components 
(see 
Annex 
2 
for 
a 
detailed description and 
Annex 
3 
for 
a 
detailed 
cost 
breakdown): 
The 
project 
has 
two 
components: 
(i) 
Community 
Agricultural 
Development 
Subprojects; 
and 
(ii) 
Capacity 
Building 
and 
Institutional 
Strengthening. 
The 
total 
project 
cost 
is 
estimated 
to 
be 
US$ 
69.99 
million 
equivalent, 
of 
which 
IDA 
will 
finance 
US$ 
56.58 
million, 
beneficiaries 
will 
contribute 
US$ 
11.82 
million, 
and Government (national and 
district 
councils) 
will 
provide 
US$ 
1.59 
million. 
The 
project 
comprises 
of 
the 
Agricultural 
Development 
Subprojects, 
benefiting 
either 
the 
community 
at 
large, 
of 
a 
village (Community 
Subprojects) 
or 
a 
group 
of 
farmers 
in 
a 
village 
(Farmer 
Group 
Subprojects) 
and 
complimentary Institutional 
Strengthening and 
Capacity 
Building 
Program 
at 
the 
-7- 
Page  14
district 
and 
community 
and 
national 
level. 
The 
subprojects 
will 
cover 
all 
aspects 
of 
community 
development 
initiatives 
such 
as 
identification, 
design, 
implementation, 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
using 
participatory 
approaches. 
The 
Capacity 
Building 
Program 
at 
the 
local level 
will 
cover 
identification, 
preparation and implementation 
of 
a capacity 
building 
program to 
support 
the 
community initiatives. 
The 
Capacity 
Building 
Program 
at 
the 
national 
level 
will 
cover 
aspects 
of 
Policy 
Planning 
and 
Regulatory 
Mechanism, 
Technology Linkages 
and 
Capacity 
for 
Project Implementation. 
Component 
1 
: 
Communitv 
Agricultural 
Development Subproiects 
This 
project 
component 
will 
consist 
of 
(a) 
Community 
Investment 
Subprojects (CIS); 
and 
(b) 
Farmer 
Group 
Investment 
Subprojects (FGIS). 
The 
aim 
of 
this 
component 
is 
to 
empower 
rural 
communities 
and farmer groups to make decisions to 
improve 
their 
economic 
well-being 
and 
to 
act 
on 
them. 
Communities and 
organized farmers' 
groups 
will 
have 
the 
primary 
responsibility 
for 
using 
participatory 
approaches 
in 
implementing 
small-scale 
investment 
activities 
supported 
by 
the 
project, 
including 
identification 
and 
planning 
of 
subprojects, 
implementation 
of 
technical 
recommendations, 
local 
procurement 
of 
inputs, 
contracting 
of 
service 
providers and 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation. 
The 
project 
will 
build 
capacity 
of 
local 
authorities, 
communities 
and farmers' groups 
by 
conducting 
tailor-made 
basic 
training 
in 
participatory 
methodologies 
such 
as 
participatory 
rural 
appraisals 
(PRA), 
subproject cycle, 
basic 
financial 
and procurement 
skills, 
participatory 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
(PM&E), 
environmental 
and social 
assessments, 
and 
HIV/AIDS 
awareness 
and 
prevention. 
A 
community 
is 
defined 
as 
a 
single 
village, 
or 
a 
significant 
portion 
thereof, 
with 
a 
common 
investment 
interest. 
Thus 
a 
community 
subproject 
will 
be 
any 
investment, 
that draws 
public 
interest 
and 
brings 
common 
benefits. 
A 
"Farmer 
Group" 
is 
defined 
as 
a 
small 
group 
(1 
0 
- 
40 
households) 
of 
the 
same 
village 
in 
which 
members 
have 
voluntarily 
agreed, 
with 
endorsement 
of 
their 
Village 
Council, 
to 
engage 
in 
an investment 
subproject that 
introduces technological innovation. 
Possible subprojects 
include: 
soil 
fertility 
and 
better 
land 
management 
(watershed 
management 
for 
soil 
and water 
conservation, 
restoration 
of 
soil 
fertility 
using 
rock 
phosphate, 
conservation and 
no 
tillage 
techniques, 
and 
fuel 
efficiency 
technologies 
- 
biogas); 
agricultural 
investments and technologies (integrated 
plant 
nutrition 
strategy 
(IPNS), 
integrated 
pest management 
(IPM), rainwater 
harvesting, 
improvement 
of 
traditional 
schemes, 
production 
of 
nontraditional 
crops 
and 
improved 
livestock); 
and 
input-output 
marketing 
(production 
of 
organic 
fertilizers, 
primary 
processing 
of 
crop and 
livestock 
products, contract 
farming 
etc.). 
Criteria 
for 
approval 
of 
subprojects 
will 
include 
gender balance, 
sustainability and 
empowerment 
of 
rural 
communities. 
In 
order 
to 
share 
risks 
involved 
in 
adopting 
new 
improved 
technologies, 
efforts 
of 
communities 
and 
farmers' 
groups 
will 
be 
complemented 
with 
direct 
transfers 
of 
financial 
resources 
to 
them (through 
local 
governments) 
on 
a matching-grant 
basis. 
This 
will 
allow 
them 
to 
shop 
and compare 
prices among 
several 
suppliers 
of 
goods 
and 
services 
needed 
to 
implement 
their 
subprojects.  Mechanisms 
for 
community-driven 
development 
will 
be 
introduced 
in 
a phased 
manner, 
to 
allow 
for 
improvements 
in 
the 
course 
of 
project 
implementation. 
Before 
implementing 
the subprojects, each 
Village Council 
or 
farmer 
group 
will 
undergo a 
participatory planning 
process 
to 
identify 
opportunities 
key 
challenges 
and 
practical 
ways 
to 
overcome 
them. 
The 
subprojects 
thus 
derived 
will 
then 
be 
costed 
and 
the 
implied 
share 
of 
the 
cost 
to 
the 
beneficiaries 
made clear. 
If 
the 
beneficiaries 
wish 
to 
undertake 
the 
project, 
it 
will 
be 
submitted 
for 
approval 
to 
the 
District 
Management 
Team 
(DMT) 
of 
the 
District 
Authority. 
With 
each 
participating 
community 
or 
farmers 
group, 
the 
cycle 
of 
subprojects 
will 
unfold 
over 
three 
years. 
The 
initial 
year 
will 
be 
devoted 
mainly 
to 
capacity 
building 
PRA, 
identification 
and 
preparation 
of 
the subprojects. 
The 
-8- 
Page  15
initial 
year 
will 
be 
devoted a 
village 
mainly to 
capacity 
building, 
PRA, 
and 
identification 
and 
preparation 
of 
the 
subprojects. 
The 
second 
and 
third 
years 
will 
be 
devoted 
to 
implementation. 
For 
CIS, 
beneficiaries 
will 
contribute 
(labor, 
materials 
or 
in 
cash) 
at 
least 17% 
of 
total 
subproject 
costs, 
excluding 
technical 
assistance 
and 
training 
and 
the 
project 
will 
contribute 
the 
difference 
up 
to 
a 
maximum 
of 
US$ 
30,000 
equivalent 
per 
village. 
Additionally 
the 
project 
will 
contribute 
100% 
of 
the 
cost 
of 
technical 
assistance 
and 
training 
up 
to 
a 
maximum 
of 
US$ 
5,000 
equivalent. 
For 
FGIS, 
the 
project 
will 
contribute 
in 
cash 50% 
of 
the 
cost 
of 
consumable 
inputs 
(seeds, 
fertilizers, 
and 
plant protection 
chemicals) 
up 
to 
US$25 
equivalent 
per 
household 
per 
year 
for 
a 
maximum 
of 
2 
years 
and 
I 
or 
80% 
of 
costs 
of 
technological innovations 
other than 
agricultural 
input 
and 
up 
to 
US$ 
8,000 
per 
village. 
In 
addition, 
the 
project 
will 
contribute 
100% 
of 
the 
cost 
of 
technical 
assistance 
and 
training 
up 
to 
a 
maximum 
of 
US$3,000 
equivalent. 
The 
total project contribution 
for 
CIS 
and 
FGIS 
subprojects 
per 
village 
will 
be 
US$46,000 
equivalent.  Farmers 
participating 
in 
the 
group 
investment 
subprojects 
will 
be 
required 
to 
have 
a 
savings account 
and 
deposit at 
least 
50% 
of 
the 
cost 
of 
consumable 
inputs 
to 
be 
purchased 
prior 
to receiving 
the 
matching contribution 
grant. 
At 
the 
end 
of 
the 
season 
they 
will 
be 
required 
again 
to 
deposit 
at 
least 50% 
of 
the 
value 
of 
inputs 
needed 
for 
the 
following 
season 
in 
order 
to 
qualify 
for 
another 
matching 
grant 
from 
the 
project. 
In 
this 
way 
farmers 
are 
expected 
to 
build 
savings 
habits 
required 
to 
purchase 
inputs 
for 
the 
following 
seasons 
and 
will 
be 
encouraged 
to 
build 
up 
relationship 
with 
financial institutions 
and 
input 
dealers. 
Component 
2: 
Capacity 
Building 
and 
Institutional 
Strengthening 
This 
component 
enhances 
the 
institutional 
and 
human 
capacity 
to 
ensure 
that 
the 
subprojects 
chosen are adequately 
considered 
in 
key 
dimensions, 
including 
environmental,  economic, 
financial 
and 
social, 
and 
implemented 
with 
acceptable 
quality. 
In 
all 
participating 
districts 
the 
project 
will 
support 
training 
to 
upgrade 
capacities 
in 
participatory 
planning 
methodologies, 
project 
implementation, 
monitoring 
and evaluation, 
financial 
and procurement 
management 
skills, 
environmental 
and 
social 
assessments, 
and 
public-private 
partnership 
in 
service 
delivery. 
Staff 
of 
the 
district 
councils 
will 
also 
undergo 
training 
to 
enable 
them 
guide 
communities 
and 
farmers' 
groups 
as 
needed. 
Technical 
assistance 
will 
be 
provided 
in 
the 
preparation 
of 
District 
Agricultural 
Development 
Plans 
(DADPs), 
which 
are 
integral 
part 
of 
the 
overall 
District 
Development 
Plans 
(DDPs). 
Under 
this 
component 
conditional 
block 
grants 
to 
the 
districts 
will 
be 
in 
cash 
in 
the 
amount 
of 
US$ 
175,000 
equivalent 
for 
three 
years. 
The 
District 
Councils 
will 
contribute 
10% 
of 
the 
grant 
(US$ 
17,500 
equivalent). 
Planning 
for 
the 
use 
of 
these 
funds 
will 
be 
the 
responsibility 
of 
the 
District 
Councils through 
the 
DMTs. 
Support 
of 
the 
project 
at 
national 
level 
will 
include 
funding 
for 
capacity 
building 
in 
key 
entities 
and 
for 
project 
preparation, 
management 
and 
implementation 
and 
additional analytical 
work 
to underpin 
ongoing 
reforms. 
The 
capacities 
of 
national 
institutions 
responsible 
for 
policy 
analysis, 
regulatory 
functions,  and 
project 
preparation, 
management 
and 
implementation 
such 
as 
planning, 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation, and 
financial 
management 
will 
be 
strengthened 
through training, technical 
assistance 
and 
provision 
of 
required 
equipment. 
The 
project 
will 
also 
finance various 
policy 
studies 
aiming 
at 
reviewing, 
harmonizing and 
rationalizing agricultural 
taxes, 
levies 
and 
fees 
on 
crop and 
livestock 
sub-sectors. 
Other 
studies 
to 
be 
supported 
will 
cover 
surveys 
of 
sectoral 
performance 
and 
beneficiary 
assessments. 
The 
project 
will 
support 
further 
development and 
updating 
of 
the 
agricultural 
sector 
monitoring 
and evaluation 
system, 
and improvement 
of 
its 
management 
information 
system 
(MIS). 
In 
addition, 
the 
project 
will 
support 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
Seed 
Act 
(1973) 
and 
the 
Plant 
Breeders 
Right 
Act 
(2002), 
which 
provide 
the 
regulatory framework 
for 
seed 
industry, 
including 
the establishment 
of 
-9- 
Page  16
Seed 
Executive Agency. 
The 
project 
will 
carry 
out 
the 
rehabilitation 
of 
four 
strategically located 
soil 
testing 
laboratories, 
provide 
the 
required laboratory 
chemicals 
and 
equipment, 
including 
soil-testing 
kits 
for 
on-site 
soil 
diagnosis. 
Finally, 
the 
project 
will 
finance 
the 
national coordination 
and

unit 
operating 
costs.

Zanzibar islands 
(Unguja

during

It

(ZPSC) 
and cleared 
by

The overall institutional 
and

arrangements have 
been prepared 
and 
appraised. 
Grant 
allocation 
for 
the 
districts 
in 
Zanzibar 
for 
the

Subprojects have 
been 
estimated at 
an 
equivalent 
of

and 
for

Building 
Program 
at 
USS0.7

The

will 
not

for

The

Environment

Cooperatives 
(MANREC). 
A 
detailed 
Zanzibar 
Project Operational 
Manual 
(ZPOM),

the 
Guidelines 
for 
the 
Preparation 
and 
Implementation 
of

Subprojects, 
a 
Zanzibar Project Implementation 
Plan 
(ZPIP) 
and 
the 
Annual 
Work 
Plan 
and 
Budget 
(AWP&B) 
for 
the 
first 
year 
of 
the 
project 
(FY 
2004) 
have 
been 
prepared 
by 
the 
Government 
of 
Zanzibar. 
These 
documents 
have been 
reviewed 
and 
steps 
to 
finalize them 
have 
been 
agreed 
at 
appraisal.

adoption 
of 
the 
ZPOM 
and 
the 
ZPIP 
and 
the

for

by

The 
IDA 
disbursements 
for 
the

and

components 
will 
follow 
the

2. 
Key 
policy 
and 
institutional 
reforms 
supported 
by 
the 
project: 
As 
noted 
above, 
the Government 
has 
committed to 
undertake 
changes 
in 
policy 
or 
renewed 
efforts 
to 
implement 
existing policies 
in 
the 
areas 
of 
trade 
of 
agricultural 
commodities, 
taxation 
of 
agricultural 
commodities 
at 
the 
local 
level, 
facilitation 
of 
marketing 
of 
agricultural 
inputs, 
and 
divestiture 
of 
agricultural 
assets 
presently 
owned 
by 
the 
state. 
The 
project 
supports 
the Government's 
commitment 
to 
decentralization 
by 
implementing 
newly 
defined 
roles 
for 
local 
governments 
in 
promoting 
rural 
growth, and 
by 
providing 
resources 
for 
improved 
capacity 
at 
the 
local 
level. 
Although 
the 
project 
does 
not 
support 
explicit 
reforms 
to promote private 
sector 
development other 
than 
those 
noted 
above, 
the 
expected increased 
volume 
of 
transactions 
in 
the 
private 
sector 
in 
participating 
communities 
will 
be 
an 
institutional 
development 
of 
considerable 
value. 
-10- 
Page  17
3. 
Benefits 
and 
target population: 
Benefits 
An 
estimated 
half 
a 
million 
smallholder 
farmers 
(with 
2 
hectares 
or 
less) 
are 
expected 
to 
benefit 
directly 
from 
increased 
rural 
incomes 
and 
improved 
food 
security, 
as 
a 
result 
of 
adopting 
improved 
agricultural 
technologies,  accessing 
new or 
improved productive 
infrastructure, 
and 
enhancing 
management 
of 
their 
soils 
and 
water 
resources. 
An 
equally 
large 
number 
of 
smallholder farmers 
is 
likely 
to 
benefit 
indirectly 
through 
the 
demonstration 
effect 
of 
repeated applications 
of 
improved productive 
technologies 
by 
participants. Through participatory 
planning 
methods, 
villages 
and 
individual 
households 
will 
benefit 
from 
increased 
awareness 
of 
other 
community-wide 
issues 
(infrastructure 
needs, 
marketing 
constraints, 
environmental 
concerns, 
prevention 
of 
HIV/AIDS) 
and 
identification 
of 
strategies 
to 
address 
them. 
In 
the 
medium 
term, 
the 
adoption 
of 
improved 
land 
and 
crop 
husbandry practices 
will 
gradually 
restore 
the 
productive capacity 
of 
soils 
of 
participating 
farmers, 
thus 
enhancing the 
sustainability 
of 
their 
existing agricultural production 
systems 
and 
reducing 
the 
pressure 
to move 
onto 
new 
and 
more fragile 
lands 
and 
protected 
areas. 
Secondary 
beneficiaries 
include 
rural 
stockists 
and 
retailers 
of 
inputs, 
agricultural 
traders, 
and 
local 
authorities 
as 
they 
increase 
their 
capacities 
to 
promote 
local 
economic 
development and 
delivery 
of 
services. 
Higher 
and 
more 
predictable 
demand 
for 
inputs 
will 
result 
in 
more 
dynamic 
and competitive 
markets 
for 
inputs 
at 
the 
local 
level. 
This 
is 
also 
expected 
to 
result 
in 
increased 
coordination 
and 
efficiency 
gains 
(through 
scale economies) 
in 
the 
overall 
input 
supply 
chains, 
with 
downward 
pressure 
on 
input 
prices. 
Target 
population 
The project 
will 
target 
an 
estimated 
500,000 
smallholder 
farmers 
in 
about 
840 
villages, 
including 
Zanzibar over 
a 
five 
year 
project 
period. 
It 
will 
cover 
26 districts 
in 
the 
main 
land 
and 
the 
islands 
of 
Unguja 
and 
Pemba 
in 
Zanzibar 
(equivalent 
to 
2 
mainland 
districts). 
The 
target number 
of 
villages 
in 
each 
district 
will 
be 
30 
in 
the 
mainland. 
In 
Zanzibar, 
a 
total 
of 
60 villages, 
spread 
over 
27 
Shehias 
(Group 
of 
villages 
for 
community 
administrative 
purposes, 
with 
each Shehia 
consisting 
of 
2 
- 
3 
villages), 
will 
be 
covered. 
The 
project implementation 
in 
each 
participating district 
in 
the 
main 
land 
will 
be 
spread 
over 
three years 
with 
the 
first 
year 
earmarked 
for 
capacity 
building, 
subprojects 
preparation 
and 
for 
project 
implementation 
in 
at 
least 2 villages. 
The 
districts 
will 
be 
brought 
into 
the 
program 
in 
a 
phased manner. 
Of 
the 
mainland 
districts, 
8 
will 
enter 
the 
program 
in 
the 
first 
year, 
followed 
by 
another 
8 
in 
year 
2, 
and 
10 
in 
year 
3. 
In 
Zanzibar, 
the 
program 
will 
begin 
during 
the 
second year 
and 
will 
be 
completed 
by 
the 
end 
of 
the 
fifth 
year. 
The 
expansion 
in 
the 
number 
of 
districts 
as 
opposed 
to 
the 
number 
of 
villages 
in 
the 
districts 
already 
brought 
under 
the 
project 
will 
be 
looked 
at 
the 
Mid-Term 
Review 
(MTR). 
The 
key 
features 
of 
the 
subprojects 
investment 
under 
PADEP 
include: 
(i) 
putting 
in 
place a 
village 
level 
micro planning 
process 
to 
allow 
members 
of 
community, especially 
the 
vulnerable 
groups, 
including 
women, 
to 
participate 
in 
planning 
and 
prioritizing 
needs; 
(ii) 
an 
elected 
community-level 
leaders 
to 
manage the 
implementation 
of 
community 
and 
farmer 
groups subprojects, 
including 
managing 
funds 
and procuring 
goods 
and 
services; 
(iii) 
systematic 
involvement 
of 
autonomous 
district 
teams 
who 
are 
trained 
in 
facilitating 
participatory 
processes, 
and 
who 
will 
work 
intensively 
with 
communities and 
farmer 
groups; 
and 
(iv) 
participatory 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
system. 
This 
has 
a 
poverty reduction 
focus 
since 
about 
70% 
of 
the 
grants 
will 
finance 
community 
subprojects, 
which 
are 
implemented 
by 
a 
large 
portion 
of 
the 
village, and 
matching contribution 
is 
primarily 
in 
kind. 
The project 
is 
also 
gender-sensitive, 
with 
women 
comprising 
at 
least 
40% 
of 
membership 
in 
subprojects, 
and 
women 
only 
farmer 
groups subprojects 
are 
allowed. 
Further, 
at 
least 
two 
signatories 
of 
subproject 
accounts 
must 
be 
women. 
In 
terms 
of 
subproject 
leadership, 
either 
the 
chair 
or 
the secretary 
of 
any 
subproject 
must 
be 
a woman.

4. 
Institutional 
and 
implementation 
arrangements: 
Implementation 
period 
The Agricultural 
Sector 
Development Program 
(ASDP) 
provides 
the 
overall framework 
for 
project 
implementation. 
The 
project 
is 
primarily 
a mechanism 
for mobilizing 
finance 
for 
implementing 
ASDP 
"Subprogram 
A" 
(Agricultural 
Sector Support 
and 
Implementation 
at 
District 
and 
Field 
Level), 
in 
particular 
Component 
A. 
1 
(Investment 
and 
Implementation), 
which 
will 
also 
be 
supported 
by 
initiatives 
of 
other 
donors, 
including 
DANIDA, 
JICA, 
EU, 
IRISH 
AID, 
FAO, 
NORAD, 
AfDB 
and 
IFAD. 
The 
project 
will 
be implemented over 
the 
period 
of 
five 
(5) 
years. 
Executing 
agencies 
Implementing 
agencies 
are 
envisaged 
at the national, 
district 
and 
village 
level. 
At 
the 
village 
level, 
the 
community/farmer 
groups, 
through 
their 
elected 
Community/Fanner 
Group 
Subproject 
Committees, 
will 
be 
responsible 
for 
preparing 
and 
implementing 
the 
Agricultural 
Development 
Subprojects. 
The 
elected subproject 
committee shall 
have 
the 
representation 
of 
at least 
40 
percent 
women. 
Specific roles and responsibilities 
of 
the 
SC 
will 
include: 
(i) 
planning 
and 
implementing 
subprojects; 
(ii) 
mobilizing 
contributions 
from 
community/farmer group 
members; 
(iii) 
providing 
information 
to 
the 
community/farmer group 
on 
implementation 
progress; 
(iv) 
handling 
management 
of 
subproject 
funds 
and 
procurement; 
(v) 
seeking 
technical 
support 
from 
public 
agencies 
and 
private 
service 
providers; 
(vi) 
preparing 
and 
submitting 
monthly, 
quarterly 
and annual 
physical 
and 
financial 
reports 
to 
the 
Village 
Council 
(VC) 
for 
submission 
to 
the 
District 
Management 
Team 
(DMT); 
and 
(vii) 
seeking 
guidance 
on 
business 
decisions 
of 
the subproject 
during 
the 
process 
of 
project 
preparation. 
At 
the 
district 
level, 
the 
District 
Executive 
Director 
(DED), 
assisted 
by 
the 
District 
Agriculture 
and 
Livestock 
Development 
Officer (DALDO), 
will 
be 
responsible 
for 
the 
project 
implementation. 
The 
DED 
will: 
(i) 
allocate 
resources 
to PADEP 
activities according 
to 
the 
approved annual 
work 
plan 
and 
budget; 
(ii) 
provide 
an 
accountant 
for PADEP 
operations 
in 
the 
district; 
(iii) 
set 
up 
agreed 
financial 
management procedures; 
(iv) 
open 
separate 
bank 
accounts for 
PADEP 
operations 
as 
required 
by 
the 
Project's 
financial 
management 
system 
and 
will 
be 
accountable 
for 
the 
funds 
received and 
released; 
(v) 
ensure 
the 
availability 
of 
the 
District's 
matching contribution 
towards 
its 
capacity 
building 
program; and 
(vi) 
procure 
project 
supplies 
according 
to 
the 
agreed procedures. 
The 
DED 
will 
appoint 
PADEP 
Desk 
Officer 
(PDO) 
to 
assist 
the 
DALDO 
on 
day-to-day 
activities 
in 
the 
field. 
At 
the 
national level, 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Agriculture 
and 
Food 
Security 
(MAFS), 
through 
its 
Directorates 
of 
Policy 
Planning 
(DPP), 
Crop 
Development (DCD), 
Research 
and Development 
(DRD), 
and 
the 
Project 
Coordination 
Unit 
(PCU) 
will 
be 
responsible 
for 
the 
coordination 
of 
the 
Institutional 
and 
Capacity 
Building 
Programs 
covering 
respectively: 
(i) 
Policy 
Planning and Regulatory 
Framework 
and 
Private 
Sector 
Performance 
(ii) 
Strengthening 
Technological 
Linkages and 
(iii) 
National 
Capacity 
Building 
for 
supporting 
project 
implementation. 
These 
MAFS 
directorates 
will 
be 
responsible 
for 
linking 
with 
other 
directorates/departments 
in 
other 
agricultural 
sector 
lead 
ministries. 
Although 
PCU 
falls 
under 
the 
Capacity 
Building 
and 
Institutional 
Strengthening 
component, 
it 
is 
responsible 
both for 
the 
overall 
coordination 
of 
the 
Project, 
as 
well 
as 
implementation 
of 
specific activities 
of 
the 
Capacity 
Building 
and 
Institutional 
Strengthening component.

Proiect coordination 
A 
National 
Project 
Steering 
Committee 
(NPSC) has 
been 
established 
by 
the 
Government 
of 
the 
United 
Republic 
of 
Tanzania 
to 
coordinate 
the 
four 
Agricultural 
Sector 
Lead 
Ministries 
(ASLM) 
and 
Zanzibar. 
The 
ASLM 
include 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Agriculture 
and 
Food 
Security (MAFS), 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Water and 
Livestock 
Development 
(MWLD), 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Cooperatives and 
Marketing 
(MCM) 
and 
the 
President's 
Office 
- 
Regional 
Administration 
and 
Local 
Government (PO-RALG). 
The 
NPSC 
has 
an 
oversight 
for 
ensuring 
that 
project 
activities 
are 
implemented 
in 
a manner 
consistent 
with 
the 
development objectives. 
Some 
of 
the 
key 
functions 
of 
NPSC 
are 
to: 
(i) 
approve 
the 
DADP 
and 
the 
Project's 
Annual 
Work 
Plan 
and 
Budget 
(AWP&B) 
for 
funding; 
(ii) 
approve 
subsequent 
amendments 
to 
the 
Project Operational 
Manual 
(POM) 
and 
Project 
Implementation Plan 
(PIP); 
(iii) 
provide 
smooth 
coordination 
and 
linkages between 
various 
sector 
ministries and 
PADEP; 
(iv) 
ensure consistence 
with 
sector 
policies 
and 
adherence 
to 
established 
norms 
and 
standards; 
(v) 
review 
quarterly 
and annual 
physical implementation 
and 
financial 
reports, 
and 
audit 
reports; 
(vi) 
oversee 
the 
process 
of 
recruiting 
key 
PCU 
staff 
through 
open 
competition; 
(vii) 
commission an 
independent 
team 
to 
undertake concurrent 
evaluation 
of 
the 
project; and 
(viii) 
articulate 
policies and 
ensure 
compliance 
during 
implementation. 
The 
NPSC 
is 
chaired 
by 
the 
Permanent Secretary, 
MAFS 
and 
has 
representation 
from 
other 
line 
ministries, 
farmer 
associations, 
private 
sector 
and 
the 
NGO 
community. 
A 
Project 
Coordinator, 
who 
is 
the 
Secretary 
to 
the 
NPSC, 
has 
also 
been appointed 
by 
the 
Government. 
The Program 
Coordinator 
of 
the 
Agricultural 
Sector 
Development 
Program 
(ASDP) 
secretariat 
and 
the 
Principal 
Secretary, 
MANREC 
in 
Zanzibar 
will 
be co-opted 
into 
the 
NPSC 
prior to 
Credit 
Negotiations. 
It 
will 
be 
a 
condition 
of 
disbursement 
against 
the 
Zanzibar 
component that a Zanzibar 
Project 
Steering 
Committee 
(ZPSC) 
to 
be chaired 
by 
the 
Principal 
Secretary, 
MANREC 
is 
established. 
The 
ZPSC 
will 
be 
responsible 
for 
ensuring that Zanzibar 
component 
is 
implemented according 
to 
the 
project 
development objectives. 
A 
Project 
Technical 
Committee 
(PTC) 
has 
been 
established 
in 
Tanzania mainland, 
with 
responsibility 
for 
coordinating 
the 
provision 
of 
advisory 
services 
and technical backstopping 
to 
the 
Project. 
It 
is 
chaired 
by 
the 
Director 
Crop 
Development, 
MAFS, 
and 
comprises heads 
of 
various 
technical 
departments 
of 
agricultural 
sector 
ministries 
and 
representatives 
from 
academic research 
institutions 
and 
independent 
authorities 
in 
the 
field 
of 
agricultural 
research, 
extension and 
policy. 
It 
will 
be 
a 
condition 
of 
disbursement 
against 
the Zanzibar component 
that 
a Zanzibar 
Project Technical 
Committee 
(ZPTC) 
is 
established. 
The 
MAFS 
will 
have an 
overall responsibility 
for 
project 
coordination, 
including 
the 
overall 
management 
of 
the 
financial 
resources, 
monitoring 
and evaluation. 
For 
this 
purpose 
the 
MAFS 
has 
established a 
Project 
Coordination 
Unit 
(PCU) 
in 
the 
ministry. 
It 
is 
fully 
staffed 
with 
agreed 
terms 
of 
reference 
for 
core 
staff 
and an 
organogram. 
The 
staffing 
of 
the 
PCU 
is 
currently 
by 
MAFS 
staff, except 
for two 
positions 
for 
which 
there 
is 
no 
expertise 
in 
the 
ministry. 
These 
positions 
have 
been 
filled 
by 
consultants. 
A 
Project 
Coordinator 
(PC) 
heads 
the 
PCU, 
and 
is 
assisted 
by 
an 
Operations 
Officer, who

that

for

The

System 
(FMS) 
will

The

(FMRs)

based

and

groups.

and

Monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
arrangements 
The 
project 
will 
use 
participatory 
methodologies that 
involve 
the 
district 
councils, 
wards, 
villages 
and farmers’ 
groups 
in 
collection, 
processing, 
and 
reporting 
of 
data 
in 
a 
simplified 
format. 
Implementing 
teams 
in 
the 
districts and 
wards 
will 
be 
trained 
in 
participatory 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
approaches. 
Baseline 
data 
collection 
will 
be 
undertaken 
as 
part 
of 
an 
extended 
PRA 
exercise 
in 
each 
targeted 
community. 
The 
baseline 
will 
include 
an 
assessment 
of 
the 
quality 
and 
availability 
of 
rural 
services 
provision, 
as 
well 
as 
current levels 
of 
production, 
asset 
ownership 
and income. 
Beneficiary-led 
assessments 
will 
be 
carried out 
by 
village 
governments 
to 
assess 
the 
benefits 
arising 
from 
subproject 
as 
they reach completion. Each 
district 
will 
summarize 
these 
assessments 
on 
an 
annual 
basis, 
in 
order 
to 
gauge 
the 
emerging 
effects 
of 
subprojects 
on 
the 
communities 
and 
to 
learn 
lessons 
for 
investments 
in 
following 
years. 
These 
district 
summaries 
will 
then 
be 
reviewed 
at 
annual 
zonal 
workshops attended 
by 
relevant 
stakeholders 
(farmers’ 
groups, 
NGOs, 
CBOs, 
private 
sector traders 
etc.). 
Project 
monitoring 
will 
be 
based 
on 
monthly 
community-prepared 
subproject reports that feed 
into 
a Management 
Information 
System 
(MIS) 
in 
every 
participating 
district. 
The 
MIS 
will 
be organized 
around 
three 
modules: 
(i) 
a 
community 
and 
farmers’ 
group 
subproject module, 
containing 
data 
on 
the 
community planning 
processes 
and 
investment 
subprojects, 
and 
the 
farm-level technology adoption and 
input 
use 
as 
well 
as 
output 
information 
and 
environmental 
monitoring; 
(ii) 
a 
financial 
management 
module, 
which 
will 
report 
on 
financial 
performance 
and 
the 
rate 
of 
subproject 
progress, 
in 
line 
with 
FMR 
requirements; 
and 
(iii) 
an 
institutional 
strengthening 
and capacity 
building 
module 
for 
district 
activities. 
Based 
on 
the 
pilot 
district 
experiences, 
a consultant 
will 
design 
the 
MIS 
and 
install 
it 
on 
district 
computers 
in 
a 
phased 
manner 
during 
project 
Year 
1. 
With 
data 
from 
the 
baseline 
surveys 
and 
beneficiary-led 
assessments, 
reports 
from 
the 
MIS, 
and 
findings 
from 
field 
visits 
(including random 
audits) 
and supervision 
reports, 
the 
districts 
will 
monitor 
project 
performance, 
identify 
implementation 
problems 
and 
achievements, 
and 
take 
appropriate actions 
to 
improve 
project 
implementation and environmental sustainability. 
The 
PCU 
will 
be 
responsible 
for 
aggregating 
district-level 
reports 
on 
a 
quarterly 
and 
annual 
basis. 
These, 
combined 
with 
the 
progress 
information on 
national-level activities, 
will 
be 
consolidated and 
submitted 
to 
the 
NPSC 
and 
to 
IDA 
by 
the 
PCU. 
After 
two 
years 
of 
project implementation, 
the 
PCU 
will 
contract 
an 
independent 
Mid 
Term 
Review 
to 
assess 
overall 
progress 
and emerging impact, 
and 
recommend 
necessary 
changes 
to project 
design 
for 
the 
remaining 
project period. 
At 
the 
end 
of 
the 
project, 
a comprehensive 
terminal 
evaluation 
will 
assess 
the 
achievement 
of 
the 
stated 
development 
objective 
- 
increases 
in 
household production, 
incomes 
and 
assets 
as 
well 
the 
environmental 
impact 
- 
by 
comparing 
the 
baseline 
and 
beneficiary 
assessments 
with 
results 
from 
a 
final 
year 
field 
survey 
of 
subprojects. 
The 
final 
evaluation 
document 
will 
provide 
the 
basis 
for 
consideration 
by 
IDA 
on: 
(i) 
whether expansion 
of 
Project activities 
should 
be 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
the 
addition 
of 
new 
districts 
or 
increase 
in 
the 
number 
of 
villages 
within 
the 
participating 
districts; 
and 
(ii) 
the 
new 
CISIFGIS 
grant, 
as 
well 
as 
District Council 
grant ceilings. 
-17- 
Page  24
D. 
Project Rationale 
1. 
Project 
alternatives 
considered 
and 
reasons 
for 
rejection: 
(a) 
Implementation 
of 
long-term Adaptable 
Program 
Loan 
(APL) 
The 
Government 
of 
Tanzania 
is 
preparing 
a comprehensive 
rural 
development 
strategy and 
program 
of 
implementation. 
PADEP 
fits 
within 
this 
framework, 
and 
the 
issues 
it 
addresses 
are 
recognized 
to 
require 
attention over 
a 
long 
time 
horizon. 
The 
use 
of 
participatory 
approaches 
and 
matching 
grants 
for 
risk 
sharing 
to 
encourage 
adoption 
of 
improved 
technologies, 
however, 
is 
somewhat 
innovative, 
and 
must 
be 
observed 
in 
practice before commitment 
is 
made 
to 
a 
longer 
term 
program. 
Therefore, 
a 
five-year 
Specific Investment 
Loan 
(SIL) 
was chosen 
instead 
of 
an 
APL. 
The 
experience 
gained 
in 
the 
course 
of 
project 
implementation 
and the 
final 
impact evaluation 
will 
provide 
valuable 
insights 
that 
could 
be 
used 
to 
prepare 
a 
long 
term 
program. 
(b) 
Self-standing 
project 
concentrating 
on 
soil fertility 
and 
agricultural 
intensijication 
Initially 
a 
proposal 
was 
presented 
to 
design 
a 
project 
focused 
on 
soil 
fertility 
and 
agricultural 
intensification. 
Through participatory 
and 
consultative 
processes, 
the 
earlier 
project 
design 
was 
rejected 
because 
of 
its 
emphasis 
on 
use 
of 
one 
input 
(Le., 
inorganic 
fertilizer) 
rather 
than an 
open 
menu 
of 
appropriate technologies. 
In 
addition, the 
original 
design 
included 
special 
allocation 
of 
foreign 
currency 
to 
support 
importation 
of 
fertilizer, 
and 
that 
was 
determined 
not 
to 
be 
consistent 
with 
the 
evolution 
of 
foreign 
exchange 
markets 
in 
Tanzania. 
Further, 
in 
the 
course 
of 
project 
preparation and 
related 
discussions 
during 
the 
finalization 
of 
the 
Agricultural 
Sector 
Development 
Strategy 
(ASDS) and 
its 
implementation 
program 
(ASDP), 
note 
was 
taken 
of 
the 
importance 
of 
participation 
of 
communities 
and 
participants 
in 
investment 
decisions. 
Therefore, the 
project 
was 
redesigned 
to 
focus 
on 
strengthening 
the 
capacity 
of 
districts 
to 
assist 
rural 
communities 
to 
identify, 
design 
and 
implement 
productive 
investment 
projects 
of 
their 
own 
choice. 
The 
spectrum 
of 
eligible 
projects 
was 
opened 
up 
to 
include 
not 
just 
those 
related to soil 
fertility, 
but 
also those 
related 
to, 
water 
resource management, 
marketing, and 
rural 
infrastructure. 
An 
earlier proposal 
to 
use 
a 
voucher 
scheme 
to 
implement 
the 
matching 
grant 
was 
rejected 
due 
to 
unnecessary 
complexity. 
(c) 
A 
broad 
rural 
development 
program 
A 
comprehensive 
Rural 
Development 
Strategy 
is 
being 
finalized 
by 
Government. 
The 
proposed 
project 
focuses 
on 
developing capacity 
of 
districts and communities 
to 
identify 
and 
implement 
demand-driven 
productive 
investments. 
By 
the 
time 
the 
Rural 
Development 
Strategy 
is 
complete 
and 
operational, 
PADEP 
will 
have 
contributed 
by 
accumulating 
experience 
in 
approaches 
that can 
be 
adapted 
to 
a 
broader 
range 
of 
activities. 
The 
RDS 
is 
a 
coordinating 
mechanism 
for 
the 
various 
sectoral 
contributions 
to 
the 
Poverty Reduction 
Strategy 
(PRS); 
Le., 
agriculture, 
water, education, 
health 
and 
rural 
roads. 
The 
actual 
implementation 
of 
individual 
sectoral 
strategies 
is 
the 
responsibility 
of 
the 
line 
ministries. 
Under 
these 
circumstances, a 
broadly defined 
sectoral development 
program 
would 
not 
be 
adequately focused 
on 
practical 
interventions 
that 
yield 
results 
on 
the ground. 
A 
decision 
was 
taken 
not 
to 
design 
an 
instrument 
providing broad 
support 
for 
implementation 
of 
the 
Rural 
Development 
Strategy, 
although 
PADEP 
is 
fully 
consistent 
with 
that 
strategy. 
(d) 
Poverty reduction 
credit 
and 
sector adjustment 
support 
Consideration 
was 
given 
to 
the 
possibility 
of 
supporting activities 
identified 
as 
part 
of 
the 
- 
18- 
Page  25
PADEP 
agenda 
through 
the 
instrument 
of 
the 
Poverty 
Reduction 
Support 
Credit 
(PRSC). 
The 
Government 
has 
determined 
that 
within 
the 
medium-term, 
IDA'S 
direct 
support 
to 
the 
agricultural 
sector 
should continue 
to 
be 
through specific 
investment 
lending 
(SIL) 
operations, 
while 
the PRSC 
will 
support 
policy 
and 
regulatory 
reforms. 
This 
is 
consistent 
with 
ASDP, 
which 
provides 
for 
basket 
funding 
as 
well 
as 
parallel 
earmarked support 
to 
its 
subprograms. 
IFAD 
and 
DANIDA 
are 
also 
supporting 
implementation 
of 
the, 
ASDP 
through 
stand alone 
projects 
aiming to 
address 
specific problems 
in 
the 
agricultural 
sector. 
Sector 
Issue 
2. 
Major 
related 
projects financed 
by 
the 
Bank 
and/or other 
development 
agencies 
(completed, 
ongoing 
and 
planned).

development agencies

(Highly Satisfactory),

(RFS)

(AMSDP)

Objective 
(DO)
