S 
The project 
will 
benefit 
from 
the 
technical 
inputs 
from 
other 
IDA 
funded 
project 
listed 
above, 
particularly 
from 
the 
extension, 
research and 
smallholder 
irrigation 
projects, 
based 
on 
demand 
by 
communities 
and 
farmer 
groups. 
PADEP 
will 
only pay 
for 
the 
incremental 
costs associated 
with 
the 
provision 
of 
specific 
services 
to 
the 
participating 
districtslcommunities. 
- 
19- 
Page  26
3. 
Lessons 
learned 
and reflected 
in 
the 
project 
design: 
An 
Operations 
Evaluation 
Department (OED) 
review 
of 
“Tanzania, 
Agriculture, 
and 
the 
World 
Bank” 
(Report 
No. 
181 
11, 
June 
30, 
1998) supports 
the 
view 
that 
‘in 
Tanzania 
at 
this 
stage 
in 
its 
development, 
the 
best 
way 
to 
eliminate 
poverty 
is 
to 
develop agriculture.’ 
The 
report 
further 
notes 
that 
for 
most 
farmers 
the 
transition 
from 
traditional 
long 
fallow, 
land-extensive, 
low-input 
subsistence 
agriculture 
to 
market-oriented 
production 
using 
substantial purchased 
inputs 
still 
lies 
in 
the 
future. 
Tanzania 
is 
at 
an earlier 
stage 
than 
some 
African 
countries 
(Kenya, 
Zimbabwe) 
in 
building 
institutions 
(markets, 
legal 
and 
regulatory framework, 
social 
and material infrastructure) 
needed 
to 
develop 
agriculture 
in 
a 
liberalized 
and 
market-oriented 
economy. 
The 
report 
also notes 
that 
agricultural 
practices 
in 
much 
of 
Tanzania 
have 
led 
to 
some 
land 
degradation 
due 
to 
depletion 
of 
nutrients 
and organic matter. 
This 
project 
responds 
well 
to 
the 
issues 
and 
lessons 
presented 
by 
OED. 
OED 
evaluation 
reports 
for 
three 
Tanzanian 
agricultural 
and 
forestry projects 
(IDA 
Credit 
Nos. 
1970, 
1994 
and 
2335) 
highlight 
two 
important 
lessons: 
(i) 
involvement 
of 
farmers 
in 
design 
and 
operation 
of 
extension programs 
is 
a 
critical 
element 
in 
ensuring 
the 
continued technical 
relevance 
and 
usefulness 
of 
the service; 
and 
(ii) 
establishment 
of 
effective 
research-extension linkages 
requires 
institutional 
reforms 
that 
create 
positive 
incentives 
for 
collaboration. 
Information 
should 
flow 
both 
ways, 
not 
only 
from 
researchers 
to 
farmers, 
but 
also 
vice 
versa. 
More 
generally, a 
review 
of 
the Bank’s experience 
in 
agricultural 
technology 
generation 
and 
transfer 
in 
several 
regions, 
as 
documented 
by 
OED 
evaluations, 
indicates that the 
following 
elements 
are 
critical for 
success: 
(i) 
beneficiary 
ownership and 
participation 
for priority 
setting, 
planning 
and 
monitoring, 
as 
well 
as 
financing; 
(ii) 
a 
single 
model 
cannot 
be 
designed 
for 
generalized 
application; 
pluralism 
and 
competition 
among 
service 
providers 
(public, 
private, non-profit, and 
non-governmental 
sectors) 
improve 
the 
efficiency 
of 
the 
system; 
(iii) 
success 
and 
sustainability 
of 
development programs 
require 
commitment 
for 
building 
social capital 
through 
organizational 
and 
technical 
training 
of 
beneficiary 
groups 
and 
community-based organizations. 
The 
international 
experience 
of 
Community 
Driven 
Development 
(CDD) 
-type 
projects, 
which 
is 
documented 
by 
various publications 
of 
the 
World 
Bank 
(e.g. 
thematic group 
on CDD) 
has 
revealed 
the 
following: 
(i) 
the 
potential 
for 
CDD 
is 
greatest 
for 
goods 
and 
services 
that 
are 
small 
in 
scale 
and 
not 
complex. 
These 
are 
activities 
within 
the 
management 
capacity 
of 
community 
organizations 
and 
that 
require 
local 
cooperation, 
such 
as 
common 
pool 
goods 
(e.g., 
surface 
water 
irrigation 
systems), 
public 
goods 
(e.g., 
rural road 
maintenance), 
and 
civil 
goods 
(e.g., 
public 
advocacy and 
social monitoring); 
(ii) 
the 
impact 
of 
CDD 
programs 
is 
directly 
related 
to 
the 
strength 
of 
the 
Community 
Based Organizations 
(CBOs) 
driving 
the 
process, 
the 
strength 
of 
the 
linkages 
with 
formal 
institutions, and 
the 
extent 
to which 
CBOs 
are 
socially inclusive; and 
(iii) 
the 
necessary 
elements 
for 
sustainability 
are: demand-based 
approach, 
articulating priorities 
defined 
by 
the 
people 
themselves 
and 
expressed 
through 
community 
contributions; social 
mobilization 
and 
the organizational capacity 
of 
local 
community 
groups; 
and 
financial viability, 
which 
entails 
significant community 
contributions 
(including 
in 
cash) 
towards 
initial 
capital 
cost 
and, 
whenever possible 
full 
recovery 
of 
operating 
and 
management 
costs. 
Further, 
the 
Bank 
has 
learnt 
that 
the 
effectiveness 
of 
CDD-type initiatives 
is 
enhanced 
by: 
(i) 
creating 
an 
enabling environment 
through 
relevant 
institutional 
and 
policy 
reform, 
including 
linking 
with 
decentralization 
process, 
developing 
sound 
sector 
policies 
and clear 
institutional 
arrangements, 
undertaking institutional 
reform 
and 
promoting 
private 
sector 
involvement 
in 
supply 
of 
goods 
and 
services; 
(ii) 
making 
investments responsive 
to 
informed 
demand 
to 
enable 
communities 
to 
make 
meaningful 
investment 
choices, 
which 
are 
affordable 
to 
them 
so 
that 
they 
can 
contribute 
to 
capital 
and 
recurrent 
costs; 
(iii) 
building 
participatory 
mechanisms 
for 
community control 
and 
stakeholder 
-20- 
Page  27
involvement 
to 
ensure 
ownership 
of 
a subproject and 
sustain outcomes; 
(iv) 
ensuring 
social 
and gender 
inclusion 
through 
understanding 
the 
existing community 
decision 
making 
processes 
and 
the 
often 
complex 
local 
political 
and social 
context; 
(v) 
investing 
in 
capacity 
building 
of 
local 
organizations, 
including 
CBOs 
and 
promoting 
use 
of 
traditional 
knowledge 
and culture-based 
skills; 
(vi) 
facilitating 
community 
access 
to information, 
including 
project 
contents 
and 
rules, 
technical 
information, 
such 
as 
accounting 
and managerial 
skills 
for 
decision 
making 
and 
implementation 
of 
activities; 
(vii) 
developing 
simple 
rules 
and 
strong incentives, supported 
by 
participatory 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
for 
advancing 
CDD 
goals 
of 
accountability, 
transparency and partnerships; 
(viii) 
maintaining 
flexibility 
in 
design 
of 
projects, 
often 
through 
piloting, to 
allow 
systems 
to 
evolve 
and better 
adapt 
to local 
demand and 
capabilities; 
(ix) designing mechanism 
for 
scaling 
up 
of 
successful 
operations 
in 
order 
to 
have greater 
impact 
on 
macro indicators 
of 
poverty; and 
(x) 
investing 
in 
an 
exit 
strategy, such 
as 
building 
local 
capacity 
to 
absorb the 
operating and 
maintenance 
costs 
of 
the 
subprojects, 
by 
encouraging 
savings 
or 
cost 
sharing. 
These 
lessons 
have 
been 
incorporated 
in 
PADEP 
design. 
Finally, 
PADEP 
project 
design 
has 
benefitted 
from 
the experiences 
of 
pilots 
in 
two 
districts, 
Morogoro 
and 
Hai, 
which 
were 
financed 
by 
the 
National 
Agricultural Extension Project 
- 
Phase 
I1 
(IDA 
Credit 
No. 
2899). 
These 
pilots 
showed 
that 
there 
is 
considerable enthusiasm among 
beneficiaries 
and 
district-level staff 
for 
the 
proposed 
approach. 
If 
sufficient 
resources 
are 
allocated 
for 
training 
and 
capacity 
building, 
the 
proposed 
approach 
is 
viable and 
promises 
to 
deliver 
the expected 
results. 
Training 
modules 
for 
PRA 
methodologies, subproject 
preparation 
and 
appraisal, 
financial 
management 
and 
procurement, 
participatory 
M&E, 
environmental 
assessment 
and 
screening, 
and 
HIV/AIDS 
awareness 
will 
be 
developed, 
and 
district 
and 
ward 
staff 
will 
be 
trained. 
4. 
Indications 
of 
borrower 
commitment 
and 
ownership: 
The 
need 
for 
it, 
in 
the 
context 
of 
rural 
development 
and 
poverty 
reduction, 
is 
specifically 
recognized 
in 
the 
CAS. 
The 
Government 
has 
set 
up 
a 
unit 
headed 
by 
an 
experienced 
officer 
of 
the 
rank 
of 
Permanent 
Secretary 
to 
prepare 
the 
project. 
The 
project 
was 
prepared 
by 
a 
team 
of 
national 
staff 
and 
consultants. 
The 
Team 
adopted 
a 
highly 
participatory 
process, 
spent 
time 
in 
the 
field 
with 
farmers, 
farmers 
organizations, 
NGOs, 
and 
district 
and 
grassroots 
level 
elected 
officials. 
An 
inter-ministerial 
committee 
with 
representation 
of 
the 
private 
sector 
has 
been 
an 
active 
participant 
in 
project 
preparation. 
Government (central 
and 
local 
level) 
has 
committed many 
high-level 
staff 
from 
several 
ministries 
and 
has 
maintained 
a continuous 
flow 
of 
counterpart 
funding 
to 
complete 
project 
preparation, 
despite 
the 
delays 
due 
to 
the 
re-orientation 
of 
the 
project. 
The 
proposal 
that 
IDA 
should 
support 
the 
project 
came 
from 
the 
Prime 
Minister 
of 
Tanzania. 
5. 
Value 
added 
of 
Bank 
support 
in 
this 
project: 
The Bank 
has 
contributed 
to 
the 
re-orientation 
of 
the 
project, 
from 
one 
focused 
on 
the 
supply 
of 
technology 
and over-reliance 
on 
Governmental 
structures 
for 
service 
delivery 
to 
one 
focused 
on 
the 
demand 
side, 
strengthening 
rural 
communities 
and 
farmer 
groups, 
and developing capacity 
at 
the 
district 
level.  Project 
design 
has 
benefitted 
greatly 
from 
best-practice 
experiences 
in 
community-driven 
approaches 
to 
rural 
development 
from 
other countries 
and 
regions. 
The 
Bank 
brings 
international 
lessons 
and 
experiences 
from 
implementing 
CDD 
projects 
in 
East, 
Central and West 
Africa, 
and 
Latin 
America. 
In 
Africa, 
the 
Bank 
has 
accumulated experience 
in 
launching, 
implementing 
and 
supervising 
subprojects, 
including 
social 
funds-type 
projects 
(TASAF 
and 
MASAF) 
and 
has 
the community-based procurement 
experience. 
The 
experience 
gained 
in 
management 
and channeling 
of 
funds 
through 
block 
grants, against 
development plans prepared 
by 
communities and 
local 
authorities, 
will 
be 
critical 
to 
the 
success 
of 
PADEP. 
-21 
- 
Page  28
The 
Bank 
has 
contributed 
to 
the 
PRSP process 
and 
is 
currently preparing 
PRSC 
to 
support 
the 
Government 
in 
its 
initiatives to 
reduce 
poverty. 
IDA'S 
support 
to 
PADEP 
will 
sustain 
the 
present 
good 
collaboration 
with 
other 
major 
development 
partners 
and make a 
significant 
contribution 
to 
the 
goals 
of 
the 
PRSP, 
particularly 
in 
the 
development 
of 
community 
driven 
initiatives 
and 
the 
decentralization 
of 
decision 
making 
and 
service 
delivery. 
In 
addition, 
IDA 
can 
provide continuity 
of 
support 
to 
scale 
up 
PADEP 
operations 
upon 
successful 
implementation 
of 
the 
first 
phase. 
The 
Bank 
has 
on-going projects 
in 
the 
agricultural 
sector 
(TARP 
11, 
NAEP 
I1 
and 
RBMSIIP) 
and 
in 
other 
sectors 
of 
the 
Tanzanian economy 
that can 
contribute technical 
solutions and 
facilitate 
institutional 
linkages 
with 
line 
ministries. 
For 
example, 
PADEP 
can 
benefit 
from 
efforts 
under 
TARP 
I1 
to 
generate 
improved 
technology, 
from 
NAEP 
I1 
in 
decentralized 
delivery 
of 
extension 
services, 
and 
from 
RBMSIIP 
in 
improved efficiency 
of 
use 
of 
water 
for 
irrigation. 
TASAF 
provides opportunities 
for 
communities 
to 
invest 
in 
subprojects 
that 
improve 
delivery 
of 
services, 
thus 
allowing 
PADEP 
to 
focus 
on 
subprojects 
intended 
to 
improve 
incomes 
directly. 
E. 
Summary 
Project 
Analysis 
(Detailed 
assessments 
are 
in 
the 
project 
file, 
see 
Annex 
8) 
1. 
Economic 
(see 
Annex 
4): 
3 
Cost 
benefit 
3 
Cost effectiveness 
0 
Other 
(specify) 
NPV=US$ 
million; 
ERR 
= 
% 
(see 
Annex 
4) 
A 
full 
quantitative evaluation 
of 
economic 
benefits 
of 
the 
project 
cannot 
be 
easily 
done 
for 
several reasons. 
Benefits 
of 
the 
component 
for 
capacity 
building 
and 
institutional 
strengthening, 
which 
includes 
mainly 
training 
and 
studies, 
are 
difficult 
to quantify 
in 
monetary 
terms. 
The 
investments 
in 
subprojects 
are 
not 
known 
ex 
ante, 
since 
they 
will 
be 
determined 
through 
the 
participatory planning 
processes. 
Benefits 
from 
the 
subprojects 
focusing 
on 
sustainable 
natural 
resource 
management 
and 
environmental conservation 
will 
accrue 
over a 
long 
time 
frame and 
in 
part 
derive 
from 
avoidance 
of 
economic 
loss (due 
to 
degradation) 
that 
is 
hard 
to 
measure. 
In 
view 
of 
these 
problems, 
the 
economic 
analysis 
is 
indicative, rather than 
precise. 
Yet 
because 
the 
project 
is 
intended 
to promote 
growth 
of 
incomes, 
economic analysis 
is 
needed 
even 
if 
it 
cannot 
be 
undertaken 
with 
precision. 
The 
economic analysis 
has 
been 
approached 
in 
three 
ways 
as 
presented 
in 
Annex 
4. 
The 
costs 
and 
benefits 
of 
adopting 
technologies 
of varying 
intensity 
in 
use 
of 
purchased 
inputs 
in 
Tanzania 
have 
been 
assessed 
in 
some 
detail. 
The 
costs 
and 
benefits 
of 
the actual choices 
made 
under 
the 
pilot 
projects 
have 
been 
evaluated 
using 
assumptions 
about 
projected 
benefit 
streams. 
Finally, 
an 
approximate 
sensitivity 
analysis 
has 
been 
undertaken 
to 
suggest 
what 
levels 
of 
income 
growth 
on 
the 
part 
of 
participating 
farmers 
and 
villages 
would 
be 
needed 
to provide 
a 
positive 
net 
present 
value 
to 
the 
project. 
The 
sensitivity 
analysis 
suggests 
that 
participating 
farmers 
and villages should realize 
incremental income 
growth 
of 
about 
1.8 
per 
cent 
annually 
in 
order 
for 
the 
project 
to 
have 
a 
positive 
net 
present value. 
If 
choices 
of 
subprojects 
properly 
incorporate an 
understanding 
of 
the 
economics 
of 
selection 
of 
technologies summarized 
in 
the 
background 
studies, 
incremental 
income 
growth 
of 
1.8 
per 
cent 
annually 
is 
a 
realistic 
expected outcome 
of 
the 
project. The 
project 
can 
therefore 
be 
expected 
to 
show 
a 
positive 
net 
present 
value. 
Details 
are 
presented 
in 
Annex 
4. 
- 
22 
- 
Page  29
2. 
Financial 
(see 
Annex 
4 
and 
Annex 
5): 
NPV=US$ 
million; 
FRR 
= 
% 
(see 
Annex 
4) 
Financial 
analysis 
of 
income-generating subprojects 
will 
be 
carried out 
annually. 
From 
the 
perspective 
of 
the participants, 
the 
financial 
analysis 
will 
differ 
from 
the 
economic 
analysis 
(summarized 
above) 
only 
to 
the 
extent that 
participants 
bear 
a 
portion, 
rather 
than all, 
of 
the 
costs 
of 
subprojects. 
Thus 
financial 
returns 
will 
be 
higher 
than 
economic, 
and 
it 
is 
for 
this 
reason that 
participants 
will 
be 
expected 
to 
save 
a 
portion 
of 
the 
incremental 
earnings 
to 
contribute 
to 
sustainability 
after 
the 
cessation 
of 
the 
grant. 
Information 
on 
the 
profitability 
of 
various subprojects 
will 
be 
made 
available 
to 
future 
participants 
to 
assist 
them 
in 
selection 
of 
subprojects. 
Fiscal Impact: 
Over 
the 
five 
(5) 
year 
period 
of 
implementation, 
the 
project 
will 
have 
only 
a 
minor 
fiscal 
impact 
on 
central 
and 
local 
governments. 
The 
central Government 
is 
expected 
to 
contribute 
only 
about 
US$ 
1.06 
million 
equivalent, 
including 
the 
form 
of 
duties 
and 
taxes, 
estimated at about 
US$ 
0.60 
million. 
The 
rest 
represents 
roughly 
10% 
of 
operating 
costs 
of 
the 
national 
coordination 
and 
facilitation 
unit. 
District 
Councils 
are 
expected 
to 
contribute 
about 
US$ 
0.53 
million 
from 
their 
own 
resources. 
District 
councils 
will 
experience 
modestly 
increased 
revenues 
from 
the 
5% 
cess 
on 
incremental marketed 
output. 
3. 
Technical: 
Technical 
considerations 
relevant 
to 
the 
project 
follow: 
Watershed management 
for 
soil 
and water conservation: 
Watershed 
management 
subprojects 
are 
undertaken 
primarily 
for 
environmental 
purposes, 
but 
they 
can 
also 
contribute 
to 
income growth.  The 
linkages 
of 
the 
watershed 
to 
generation 
of 
runoff 
provide 
for 
the 
necessary 
integration 
of 
water 
quantity 
management 
with 
planning 
for 
water 
quality 
and 
control 
of 
erosion 
and 
sedimentation. 
Examples 
of 
watershed 
management 
for 
soil 
and water 
conservation subprojects 
to 
be 
supported 
by 
PADEP may 
include 
construction 
of 
contours, 
prevention 
of 
gullies, 
construction 
of 
terraces, 
agro-forestry, 
and 
others. 
Conservation 
tillage: 
Added 
crop 
residue 
and 
minimal 
tillage 
reduce 
runoff 
of 
soil 
and 
agro-chemicals, 
and 
thereby 
protect 
the 
quality 
of water 
in 
the 
ecosystem. 
Conservation 
tillage 
decreases 
erosion, 
improves 
water 
quality, 
enhances 
retention 
of 
moisture 
in 
the 
soil, 
improves water 
infiltration, 
decreases 
soil 
compaction, 
improves 
tilth, 
enhances 
biological 
activity, 
and 
reduces release 
of 
carbon 
gases. 
Reducing 
and/or 
eliminating tillage 
helps 
increase 
soil 
fertility. 
Examples 
of 
conservation 
tillage 
subprojects that 
will 
be 
supported 
by 
PADEP 
include improved 
fallows, 
use 
of 
cover 
crops, 
use 
of 
farm 
implements 
for 
soil 
and 
water conservation, practices 
to 
control soil 
erosion, 
and 
use 
of 
green 
manure. 
Eficient 
use 
of 
fertilizers: 
Participants can 
use 
resources 
under 
PADEP 
to 
make 
more 
efficient 
use 
of 
fertilizers. 
In 
phosphorus-deficient 
soils, 
Minjingu 
rock 
phosphate 
(MRP) exploited 
from 
Manyara 
Region 
near 
Lake 
Manyara 
is 
as 
effective 
and 
profitable 
as 
imported 
Triple 
Super Phosphate 
(TSP).  Farmers 
are 
applying 
125-250 
kg 
P/Ha 
as 
a 
capital 
investment, and 
expect 
a 
five-year residual 
effect. 
Further, 
nitrogen 
and 
phosphorous 
applied 
in 
combination 
have 
resulted 
in 
significantly 
higher 
yields. 
Other 
subproject 
activities 
that 
may 
be 
supported 
by 
PADEP 
include 
use 
of 
organic manure 
in 
combination 
with 
mineral 
fertilizers and 
production 
and 
use 
of 
bio-fertilizers. 
Local 
research shows 
that 
the 
application 
of 
organic 
manures 
in 
combination 
with 
mineral fertilizer 
has 
given 
higher 
crop 
yield 
increases 
than when 
both 
are 
applied 
separately. These 
kind 
of 
subprojects 
have 
the 
potential 
to 
increase 
- 
23 
- 
Page  30
and 
sustain 
production 
levels, 
enhance 
the 
economic 
potential 
of 
a 
production 
system, 
and counteract and 
minimize 
environmental 
pollution. 
Fuel 
efficient 
technology: 
Biogas 
is 
one 
of 
the 
fuel-efficient 
technologies that 
participants 
may 
choose 
to 
adopt 
with 
support 
of 
PADEP 
resources. 
The 
main 
positive 
impacts 
of 
biogas 
technology 
include: 
(i) 
conservation 
of 
the 
forest 
resource 
as 
a 
result 
of 
decreased 
use 
of 
fuel 
wood (with 
resulting 
increase 
in 
carbon 
sequestration); 
(ii) 
renewable 
energy 
used 
for 
lighting 
and cooking; 
(iii) 
reduced 
workload for 
women 
and 
girls; 
(iv) 
cost savings 
over 
kerosene; 
and 
(v) 
improved 
nutrient 
cycling 
with 
lower 
emissions 
to 
the 
atmosphere. 
Biogas 
technology 
also 
produces 
slurry 
for 
conditioning 
of 
soil. 
Integratedplant nutrition 
techniqueshtrategies 
(IPNS): 
The 
strategy 
optimizes 
all 
aspects 
of 
nutrient 
cycling 
- 
supply, 
uptake, 
and 
loss 
to 
the 
environment 
- 
to improve 
food 
production. 
At 
farm 
level, 
IPNS 
aims 
to optimize 
the 
productivity 
of 
nutrient 
flows 
that 
pass 
through 
the 
farming 
system 
during 
a 
crop rotation. 
This 
means 
application 
of 
external 
plant 
nutrient 
sources 
and 
additives, 
efficient 
processing, 
and 
recycling 
of 
crop 
residues 
and 
on-farm 
organic 
wastes 
to 
minimize 
plant nutrient 
losses. 
In 
the 
process, 
IPNS 
empower farmers 
by 
increasing 
their 
technical 
know-how 
and decision-making 
capacity, and 
promote 
changes 
in 
land 
use, 
crop rotation, 
and 
interactions among 
forestry, 
livestock 
and 
cropping 
systems 
in 
support 
of 
agricultural 
intensification. 
At 
the 
village or farming community 
level, 
IPNS 
take 
into 
account 
plant 
nutrient 
sources 
outside cropped 
areas, 
including 
those 
in 
irrigation 
water 
and 
flood 
sediments, 
livestock 
manure, 
and 
forest 
litter 
and organic 
material 
that 
is 
physically 
transferred 
from 
forest and 
pastures. 
IPNS 
promotes 
rationalization 
in 
the 
transfer 
of 
organic matter and 
plant 
nutrients 
from 
non-cropped 
to 
cropped 
areas, 
and 
the 
mobilization 
of 
unused 
nutrient 
resources. 
It 
also 
saves 
valuable 
nutrient 
sources 
oftenly 
diverted 
as 
domestic 
fuel, 
raw 
materials 
for 
building 
or for 
industrial 
purposes. 
Integratedpest 
management 
(IPM): 
According 
to 
the 
definition 
by 
FAO, 
IPM 
is 
an 
approach 
to 
pest management 
that 
in 
the 
context 
of 
the 
associated 
environment 
and 
the 
population 
dynamics 
of 
pest 
species, 
utilizes 
all 
suitable techniques and methods 
in 
as 
compatible 
a manner 
as 
possible and maintains 
pest 
population 
at 
levels 
below 
those 
causing 
economically 
unacceptable damage 
or 
loss. 
IPM 
integrates 
multiple 
instruments 
(chemical, 
biological, 
cultural, 
mechanical) 
for 
management 
of 
multiple 
pests 
(insects, 
weeds, 
disease 
pathogens, nematodes, vertebrates, etc). 
IPM 
incorporates 
environmental and 
social 
concerns. 
The 
main 
goals 
of 
IPM 
are 
to 
sustain 
natural 
resources 
over 
the 
long 
term, 
to 
make 
more 
rational 
use 
of 
pesticides, 
to 
reduce 
environmental contamination 
and 
costs, 
to utilize 
natural 
biological 
controls, 
to 
minimize 
pesticides 
resistance 
problems, 
and 
to improve 
safety 
of 
food 
and workers. 
Within 
the 
context 
of 
IPM, 
the 
project 
will 
promote 
and support 
safe 
use 
of 
pesticides 
in 
combinations 
with 
observation, 
preventive 
and 
intervention 
methods 
in 
crops, 
particularly 
vegetable 
and 
fruit 
production. 
Use 
of 
rain 
harvesting 
techniques: 
Rains 
of 
high 
intensity often 
cause 
devastating effects 
on 
the 
environment, 
particularly 
in 
areas 
with 
little 
or 
no 
vegetation. 
Rainwater harvesting 
methods 
can 
help 
to 
reduce 
or 
prevent 
the 
damage 
from 
heavy 
rains. 
Surface catchment 
is 
the 
most effective 
among 
the 
rainwater 
harvesting methods that 
could 
mitigate 
the 
possible 
environmental 
hazards caused 
by 
rain. 
The 
project 
will 
therefore support subprojects that 
aim 
at 
harvesting rainwater 
for 
irrigation, 
domestic 
and 
livestock 
use, 
such 
as 
chaco 
dams 
and 
water 
bunds 
in 
rice 
irrigation. 
Improvement 
of 
traditional 
irrigation 
schemes: 
The 
project 
will 
also 
support 
the 
rehabilitation 
of 
weirs, 
irrigation 
canals 
and 
construction 
of 
diversion 
boxes 
to improve 
efficiency 
of 
water 
use 
on 
irrigated 
lands.  One 
of 
the 
major 
causes 
of 
crop 
failure 
and 
low 
productivity 
is 
moisture 
stress. 
By 
supporting 
traditional 
irrigation 
and 
rehabilitating 
drainage structures, 
the 
project 
can 
help 
communities 
to 
address 
this 
problem.  Experience 
shows 
that 
with 
efficient 
irrigation 
scheme 
two 
crops can 
be 
harvested 
on 
the 
same 
piece 
of 
land, 
thus 
increasing 
productivity 
per 
unit 
area. 
In 
addition 
to 
open canal 
- 
24 
- 
Page  31
irrigation, 
other 
types 
of 
systems, 
including 
drip 
and treadle 
pumps, 
will 
be 
supported 
at 
the request 
of 
communities 
and 
groups. 
Processing and 
marketing 
of 
agricultural 
products: 
PADEP 
will 
also, at 
the 
request 
of 
participants, 
support subprojects 
to 
improve 
ago-processing,  such 
as 
small-scale 
milling 
of 
oilseeds, 
rice, 
and 
maize, processing 
of 
cashew 
nuts, 
canning and 
preserving 
of 
fruits 
and 
vegetables, 
and 
processing 
of 
dairy 
products. 
The project 
will 
also support 
the 
establishment 
of 
village marketing 
centers, 
construction 
of 
market 
yards, 
and 
small 
facilities 
for 
storage 
of 
grain. 
Communities and 
farmers groups 
may 
elect 
to 
use 
project 
resources 
to 
enter 
into 
relationships 
of 
contract farming. 
4. 
Institutional: 
Institutional 
capacity 
building 
is 
an 
integral part 
of 
the 
project 
activities 
since the 
project 
will 
be 
implemented 
under 
a 
highly 
decentralized 
settings, 
by 
the 
district 
councils and 
village 
communities. 
4.1 
Executing 
agencies: 
region. 
They 
have 
elected 
officials, 
levy 
taxes, 
and 
manage 
communal 
accounts. 
They 
are 
in 
a 
position 
to 
work 
with 
district 
level 
officers 
in 
the 
identification 
of 
problems, 
definition 
of 
priorities, 
and 
implementation 
of 
community 
investment 
subprojects. 
There 
is 
a 
wide 
range 
of 
farmers’ 
groups 
and 
producer and 
financial 
organizations, 
including 
Savings 
and 
Credit 
Societies 
in 
rural 
Tanzania. 
The 
decentralized 
system 
of 
governance 
has 
empowered 
District 
Councils to 
carry out 
planning 
and 
implementation 
of 
their 
developmental 
activities. 
They 
are 
permitted 
to 
generate 
their 
own 
revenue 
by 
levying 
local 
taxes 
within 
limits 
set 
at 
the 
national level. 
Their 
establishment 
costs, 
primarily 
staff 
salaries, 
are 
funded 
by 
the 
Central 
Government 
through 
grants. 
Districts 
are 
expected 
to 
use 
their 
revenues 
to 
meet 
their 
incremental 
recurrent 
costs 
and developmental 
costs. 
The 
project 
will 
support 
both 
increased 
fiscal 
and 
administrative decentralization 
by 
empowering 
district, 
community 
and farmer 
groups 
to 
plan 
developmental activities, 
to 
budget, 
and 
to 
manage 
conditional 
grants. 
Village 
governments 
in 
Tanzania 
are 
among 
the 
best 
organized 
rural 
local 
governments 
in 
the 
4.2 
Project 
management: 
will 
be 
headed 
by 
the 
District 
Executive Director 
(DED), 
and 
will 
include 
the 
District 
Agricultural 
and 
Livestock 
Development 
Officer (DALDO), 
the 
District 
Planning 
Officer 
(DPO),  the Treasurer, 
the 
Community 
Development 
Officer 
(CDO) 
and 
the 
Cooperative and 
Marketing 
Development 
Officer 
(CMDO). 
The 
DMT 
will 
be 
responsible 
for 
translating 
the 
project 
concepts 
and 
strategies 
into 
activities 
and 
results. 
They 
will 
be 
responsible 
for motivating 
and 
guiding 
the 
field 
level 
functionaries 
(district 
extension 
services 
and 
NGOs), 
who 
in 
turn 
will 
assist 
the 
farmer 
groups 
to 
access 
the 
services 
financed 
by 
the 
project. 
The 
district 
level 
team, 
including 
representatives 
from 
NGOs, 
farmers’ organizations and 
other 
local 
stakeholders, 
will 
be 
responsible 
for 
preparing 
detailed 
work 
plans, 
and 
for 
periodic 
monitoring 
and 
reporting to 
the 
regional 
and 
central authorities. 
Similarly, 
the 
management 
of 
institutional 
strengthening and 
capacity 
building 
programs 
at 
the 
national 
level 
will 
be 
entrusted 
to 
MAFS. 
Project 
management 
will 
be 
at 
the 
level 
of 
the 
District. 
The 
District 
Management 
Team 
(DMT) 
The 
PCU 
at the 
central 
level 
will 
have 
overall responsibility 
for 
project 
management, 
coordination, 
monitoring, through 
the 
Regional 
Administrative 
Secretaries 
(US) 
in 
the 
country, 
and 
for 
securing 
and/or 
providing 
support 
services 
at 
the 
national 
level. 
A 
Project Coordinator 
will 
serve 
as 
the 
head 
of 
the 
PCU, 
The 
line 
functional responsibility 
will 
be 
delegated 
to 
technical 
experts. 
The 
PS 
- 
MAFS 
will 
chair 
the 
NPSC 
and 
will 
be 
the 
Accounting 
Officer 
of 
the 
project 
funds. 
The 
PS-MAFS 
will 
- 
25 
- 
Page  32
receive 
the 
Central 
Government 
budget 
allocation 
for 
the 
project 
under 
his 
or 
her 
ministry’s 
vote 
and 
will 
ensure 
adequate 
implementation 
and 
accountability 
of 
project 
proceeds. 
4.3 
Procurement 
issues: 
The 
Tanzania Government 
has 
long 
realized 
the 
importance 
of 
public 
procurement 
to 
the 
economic 
development 
of 
Tanzania 
and 
hence 
to 
the 
fulfillment 
of 
key 
objectives 
within 
the 
national 
Poverty 
Reduction 
Strategy. 
To 
this 
effect, Tanzania 
was one 
of 
the 
first 
countries 
in 
Sub-Saharan 
Africa 
to 
enact 
a 
law 
modeled 
on 
the 
UNCITRAL 
model 
law. 
Since 
1996, 
when 
the 
first 
CPAR 
was 
carried 
out, 
the 
Government 
has 
been 
working 
closely 
with 
the 
World 
Bank 
and 
other development 
partners 
to 
enhance 
the 
economy 
and 
efficiency 
of 
its 
procurement 
system 
and 
to 
make 
it 
more 
transparent 
and 
accountable. 
The 
January, 2003 
assessment 
of 
the 
procurement 
system 
in 
Tanzania 
looked 
at 
all 
areas 
of 
public 
procurement 
operations, 
including 
the 
legislative framework, 
the 
performance 
of 
regulatory 
functions, 
the 
enforcement 
regime, 
the 
capacity 
of 
public 
sector 
institutions 
to 
conduct 
procurement, and 
the 
effects 
of 
corruption 
on 
procurement. 
It 
formed 
an 
integral part 
of 
the 
World 
Bank’s activities 
in 
Tanzania 
as 
presented 
in 
the 
Country 
Assistance Strategy 
(CAS) 
of 
2000 
and 
serves 
a 
number 
of 
its 
key 
themes, 
particularly 
higher 
economic 
growth, 
poverty reduction 
and 
institutional 
reforms 
to improve 
governance. 
The 
CPAR’s 
key 
findings 
are 
summarized 
in 
the 
following 
paragraphs. 
Despite 
the 
economic 
benefits 
mentioned 
above, 
procurement 
has 
not 
to 
date 
been 
considered 
to 
be 
a core 
function 
of 
government, 
worthy 
of 
specialized 
staff 
and dedicated 
organizational 
units. 
The 
legal 
framework 
for 
public 
procurement 
in 
Tanzania 
is 
in 
transition. 
While 
some 
very important 
steps 
in 
the 
right 
direction 
have 
been 
taken 
with 
the 
introduction 
of 
the 
procurement 
Act 
in 
July 
200 
1, 
there 
remain 
some 
critical 
outstanding 
issues, 
particularly 
concerning 
local 
government procurement, 
access 
to 
complaint 
and 
dissemination 
of 
legal 
texts. 
Overall, 
and compared 
to 
other countries 
in 
the 
region, the 
legal framework 
for 
public 
procurement 
is 
updated and 
adequate. 
The 
Local 
Government procurement 
Regulations 
have 
recently 
been 
issued, 
based 
on 
the 
Procurement 
Act 
of 
2001. 
These 
will 
clear 
the 
legal 
confusion 
and 
contribute 
to reduction 
of 
malpractices 
in 
local 
government procurement 
system. 
Currently, 
aggrieved 
bidders 
have 
very 
limited 
access 
to 
a 
complaints 
mechanism. 
The 
Bank 
has 
recommended 
to 
the Government that a 
credible complaints mechanism should 
be 
introduced 
by 
strengthening 
the 
complaints 
handling 
ability 
of 
the 
Regulatory 
Body 
and 
establishing 
the 
Public 
Appeals 
Authority, 
already entrenched 
in 
the 
legislation, 
to 
provide 
a 
functioning 
avenue 
of 
complaints 
for 
bidders. 
The 
single 
most 
important 
issue 
on 
the 
legal framework 
is 
the 
lack 
of 
dissemination and proper 
application 
of 
the 
rules. 
After 
a 
period 
of 
carving out 
the 
new 
rules, 
the 
country 
is 
now 
focusing 
on 
making 
those 
rules 
work, 
by 
developing 
enforcement 
mechanisms. 
The 
knowledge 
of 
procurement 
rules 
and capacity 
to 
apply 
these 
rules 
is 
very 
poor 
in 
most 
of 
the 
procuring 
entities.  Procurement audits 
are 
seldom 
performed 
and 
the 
consequences 
for 
breach 
of 
procurement 
rules, 
outside 
corruption 
cases, 
are 
minimal. 
As 
a 
consequence 
hereof, enforcement 
of 
the 
procurement 
rules 
suffers 
and 
malpractices 
in 
direct 
conflict 
with 
existing 
rules 
are 
allowed 
to 
flourish. 
Securing 
bidders 
and the 
public 
access 
to 
information 
is 
one 
of 
the 
cornerstones 
of 
sound 
procurement 
practices. 
Procuring 
entities 
are 
presently 
utilizing 
a 
number 
of 
different 
media 
for 
advertising 
procurement 
opportunities, 
making 
access 
to 
information 
and 
enforcement 
of 
the 
rules 
on 
advertisement 
difficult. 
The 
Bank 
has 
recommended 
to 
the 
Government 
that a 
central 
media 
for 
publication 
of 
procurement 
information, 
a Procurement 
Journal, 
should 
be 
established, 
supplemented 
with 
an 
Internet 
information 
site. 
-26- 
Page  33
At 
present, 
the 
Central 
Tender 
Board 
(CTB) 
holds 
both 
executive and 
regulatory 
powers, 
which 
contributes 
to 
an 
unstable 
situation 
where 
the 
CTB 
on 
one 
hand 
participates 
in 
the 
procurement 
procedure 
and 
on 
the other 
hand 
monitors 
the 
same 
procedures. 
The 
executive 
role 
of 
the 
CTB 
also leads 
to 
lack 
of 
accountability 
and 
responsibility 
at 
the 
level 
of 
the 
ministerial 
tender 
boards and 
the 
ministerial 
Accounting 
Officers, 
who 
are 
otherwise responsible 
for 
the 
budget 
but 
not 
for 
procurement 
under 
that 
body. 
Due 
to 
the 
broad 
procurement 
issues 
raised 
above 
and capacity 
assessment 
carried out 
for 
the 
MAFS, 
the 
project’s 
procurement 
risk 
is 
rated 
as 
“high”. 
The project, 
however, 
is 
addressing 
these 
issues 
by 
implementing 
the 
following 
strategies: 
(i) 
building 
the 
capacity 
of 
Ministerial 
Tender 
Board 
(MTB) 
secretariat 
staff 
to 
handle procurement 
of 
goods, 
works 
and consultants 
using 
IDA 
procurement 
procedures; 
(ii) 
recruiting 
a 
Procurement Specialist 
with 
qualifications and 
experience 
acceptable 
to 
the 
Bank to 
work 
in 
the 
PCU 
for 
a 
period 
of 
least 
two 
years, 
who 
besides 
carrying out 
procurement 
responsibilities, 
shall 
also 
conduct on-the-job 
training to 
Supplies 
Officers 
at 
MAFS 
and 
in 
the 
district 
councils; and 
(iii) 
use 
of 
the Procurement 
Manual 
that 
meets 
the 
requirements 
of 
the 
Bank 
Guidelines, 
the 
Public 
Procurement 
Act 
(2001), 
the 
Local 
Government Procurement 
regulations 
and 
Community-based 
Procurement. 
4.4 
Financial 
management 
issues: 
The 
Country Financial 
Assessment 
Review 
(CFAA) 
completed 
in 
May 
2001, 
identified 
a 
number 
of 
weaknesses 
that 
need 
to 
be 
addressed 
to 
enhance 
public 
financial 
management. 
Major 
fiduciary 
weaknesses 
identified 
include: 
(i) 
non-compliance 
to 
fiduciary 
requirements; 
(ii) 
inadequate 
human 
resource 
capacities; 
(iii) 
inadequate 
budgetary 
processes 
and 
monitoring; 
and 
(iv) 
inadequate 
public 
sector accounting, 
reporting 
and 
auditing 
systems. 
The 
government 
has 
agreed 
to 
address 
these 
weaknesses 
through 
implementation 
of 
the 
revised 
Public Financial 
Management 
Reform 
Program 
(PFMRP), 
which 
is 
supported 
by 
several 
bilateral 
donors, 
beginning 
FY04. 
The 
revised 
PFMRP 
will 
be 
a 
roadmap 
to 
built 
an 
effective 
and 
sustainable 
financial 
accountability 
framework 
in 
the 
public 
sector, 
by 
strengthening government accounting, 
reporting, 
auditing 
and 
accountability 
systems. 
In 
the 
recent 
years, 
a 
significant 
improvement 
in 
public 
sector 
accounting and 
reporting 
system 
has 
been 
achieved. 
Most 
notably, 
the government 
has 
put 
in 
place 
a 
sound 
financial 
management 
system, 
with 
the 
introduction 
of 
a 
computerized Integrated 
Financial 
Management System (IFMS),  and 
the 
enactment 
of 
the 
new Public 
Finance 
Act 
No. 
6 
of 
2001, 
and 
the 
Public 
Procurement 
Act 
No. 
3 
of 
2001. 
For 
the 
first 
time 
the 
annual 
public 
accounts 
of 
the government 
have 
been 
produced 
within 
the 
statutory 
period. 
In 
addition, 
the 
government 
has 
undertaken 
a 
number 
of 
actions 
to 
strengthen the 
accounting 
and 
financial reporting 
fbction 
within 
Ministries, 
Departments and Agencies 
(MDAs). 
These 
include: 
(i) 
finalization 
of 
an 
operation and accounting 
procedures 
manuals; 
(ii) 
implementing 
measures 
to 
control 
all 
commitments 
at 
central 
government 
level, 
including 
non-Local 
Purchasing 
Order (LPO) 
commitments; 
(iii) 
Accounting Officers 
(AOs) have 
signed letters 
of 
appointment 
and 
have 
received 
training 
in 
their 
role 
and responsibilities according 
to 
the 
Public 
Finance 
Act; 
(iv) 
AOs 
have 
now 
direct 
access 
to 
IFMS; 
and 
(v) 
Audit 
Committees 
have 
been 
established 
in 
all 
MDAs. 
- 
27 
- 
Page  34
The 
Local 
Government 
Reform 
Program 
(LGRP) 
Progress 
report 
for 
the 
period 
of 
July to 
December 
2002, 
indicates that 
some 
steps 
have 
been undertaken 
to 
strengthen the 
Local 
Government 
financial 
management 
systems. 
These 
include: 
(i) 
the 
assessment 
of 
the 
financial 
management 
capacity 
in 
all 
districts 
Local 
Government 
Authorities 
(LGAs); 
(ii) 
the 
use 
of 
new 
guidelines 
for 
preparation 
of 
development plans 
and 
budgets; 
and 
(iii) 
use 
of 
new 
chart 
of 
accounts 
under 
the 
Government 
Financial 
Statistics 
(GFS) codes 
by 
all

5.5 
What 
mechanisms have 
been 
established 
to monitor 
and 
evaluate

of

Do

Environmental 
monitoring 
has 
been 
designed 
to 
ensure 
that 
mitigation 
measures 
are 
implemented. 
Each 
EMP 
will 
demonstrate 
how 
identified 
impacts 
are 
matched 
with 
mitigation 
measures 
and monitoring 
plans. 
The 
monitoring plan 
will 
use 
the 
findings 
of 
existing 
baseline 
data, 
or 
the 
recommendations 
for 
new 
baseline 
data 
to 
fill 
gaps, 
as 
the 
means 
to 
measure 
the 
progress 
and 
problems 
in 
compliance 
with 
the 
GOT 
and 
WB 
Safeguard 
Policies. 
An 
effective 
monitoring 
plan 
will 
consist 
of 
the 
following 
elements: 
monitoring 
objectives; 
description 
of 
performance 
indicators that 
provide 
linkages 
to 
impacts and 
mitigation 
measures 
identified 
in 
EA; 
description 
of 
parameters 
to 
be 
measured, 
methods 
- 
30 
- 
Page  37
to 
be 
employed, 
sampling 
locations, frequency 
of 
measurements, 
detection 
limits 
(where 
appropriate) 
and 
definition 
of 
thresholds that 
will 
signal 
the 
need 
for 
remedial 
actions; 
institutional 
responsibilities, 
timing 
and timetable 
for 
monitoring; reporting 
arrangements (to 
the 
NEMC 
and 
the 
Bank); 
and 
cost 
and 
financing 
provisions. 
Environmental 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
will 
be 
incorporated 
in 
all 
PADEP 
supported 
subprojects.  Because 
PADEP 
subprojects 
are 
small and most 
of

will

beneficiary-led environmental

will 
be 
used

will

a 
small 
number 
of 
key 
indicators 
that can

easily

by 
members 
of 
the 
community.

Sustainability 
and

1. 
Sustainability: 
Institutional 
sustainability 
- 
Institutional 
sustainability 
will 
derive 
from 
capacity 
building 
and 
local 
ownership 
of 
the 
subprojects. 
PADEP 
implementation 
arrangements 
are 
built 
into 
the 
structures 
of 
local 
government 
(district 
councils) 
and 
are 
consistent 
with 
the 
on-going 
Local 
Government 
Reform 
Program 
(LGRP), 
which 
is 
also 
supported 
by 
IDA. 
Financial 
sustainability 
- 
Financial 
sustainability 
will 
derive 
from 
the 
stream 
of 
eamings 
generated 
by 
the 
subprojects, 
either 
in 
cash 
or 
in 
kind. 
The 
project 
design 
has 
built 
in 
incentives 
for 
savings 
by 
farmers' 
groups. 
This 
mandatory saving 
in 
a group's 
bank 
account 
of 
a 
certain 
portion 
of 
the 
revenue 
generated, 
to 
finance purchased 
inputs 
for 
the 
next farming 
season, 
will 
enable 
households 
to 
sustain 
adopted 
new 
technologies 
beyond 
the 
project 
life. 
Some 
of 
the 
subprojects, such 
as 
dip 
facilities, 
may 
charge 
"user 
fees" 
and 
deposit 
these 
in 
the 
operations 
and 
maintenance 
(O&M) 
account. 
For 
smallholder 
- 
32 
- 
Page  39
irrigation 
subprojects, 
the

to

on

a 
certain 
agreed

of

to

Environmental and social sustainability 
- 
The 
PADEP 
design 
has 
integrated environmental 
appraisal, 
monitoring 
and evaluation 
as 
part 
of 
the 
subproject 
cycle 
management. 
This 
will 
enable 
participating 
districts, 
communities and 
farmers' groups 
to 
assess 
environmental 
risks 
and 
address 
them 
in 
a 
timely 
and 
practical 
manner. 
The 
EMP 
will 
be 
prepared 
for 
each 
subproject 
as 
part 
of 
the appraisal 
process. 
PADEP 
will 
build 
the 
capacity 
of 
MAFS' 
environmental 
unit 
and 
district 
staff 
to 
carry 
out environmental 
assessment 
and 
monitoring 
and evaluation 
of 
subprojects. 
Guidelines 
for 
environmental 
and social 
assessment 
will 
be 
translated 
into Kiswahili 
and 
distributed 
to 
all 
participating 
districts and 
rural 
communitieshillages.

of

-

and administrative

now

and

will 
facilitate 
more 
sustainable 
supply.

Controversial 
Aspects: 
The 
use 
of 
matching 
grants 
for 
adoption 
of 
technology 
was 
initially 
considered controversial, 
because 
the 
grant 
was 
misunderstood 
to 
be 
a 
subsidy 
for 
fertilizer. 
After 
extensive 
consultation 
the 
purpose 
of 
the 
grant 
is 
better 
understood, 
and 
the 
concept 
is 
now 
widely 
supported. 
In 
short, 
the 
matching 
grants 
are 
provided 
to 
(i) 
introduce 
new 
technologies; 
(ii) 
share 
the 
risk 
of 
innovation; 
(iii) 
jump 
start 
a 
demonstration effect; and 
(iv) 
strengthen 
farmer group organizations.

Project Description

I 
A:

rm 
e n t Project 
This 
Annex 
is 
condensed 
from 
the 
project 
documentation 
and 
working 
papers 
which 
describe 
each 
project 
component 
in 
greater 
detail, 
and 
which 
comprise 
part 
of 
the 
Project Implementation 
Plan 
(PIP). 
Project 
costs 
include 
IDA 
and 
counterpart 
financing 
from 
central and 
local 
governments, 
as 
well 
as 
beneficiaries 
and 
all 
contingencies. 
By 
Component: 
Project Component 
1 
- 
US$50.51 
million 
Community 
Agricultural 
DeveloDment 
Subproiects 
This 
component 
will 
assist 
the 
government 
to provide matching 
grants 
to 
communities and 
farmers’ groups 
to 
finance 
agricultural 
development subprojects 
selected 
through 
PRA 
and 
approved 
by 
the 
Village 
Councils. 
Under 
this 
component, 
the 
project 
will 
support 
both 
Community 
Investment 
Subprojects 
(CIS) 
and Farmer 
Groups 
Investment 
Subprojects 
(FGIS). 
The 
project 
will 
support at least 
one 
community 
subproject and 
at 
least 
four 
farmers’ 
groups subprojects 
in 
each 
of 
the 
participating 
villages. 
It 
is 
projected 
that 
over 
a five-year 
period 
the 
project 
will 
finance a 
total 
of 
about 840 
community 
subprojects 
and 
at 
least 
3,360 
farmers’ groups 
subprojects, 
including 
in 
Zanzibar; about 
26 
districts 
in 
the 
mainland, 
plus 
the 
Pemba 
and 
Unguja 
islands 
in 
Zanzibar 
are 
expected 
to 
participate 
under 
this 
project, 
with 
a 
maximum 
of 
30 
village 
from 
each 
district. 
This 
projection 
is 
based 
on 
the 
number 
of 
districts and villages 
expected 
to 
participate 
in 
the 
project 
in 
each year 
of 
implementation. 
A 
subproject 
will 
by 
design, 
be 
simple 
to 
enable 
the 
community 
or 
farmer group 
to 
participate 
in 
its 
implementation 
and 
subsequent maintenance. 
An 
individual 
subproject 
will 
be 
supported 
for 
one 
to 
three 
years, 
depending 
on 
the 
nature 
of 
activities. 
All 
subprojects 
will 
be 
location-specific. 
(i) 
Farmer 
Group Investment 
Subprojects 
(US% 
16.11 
million) 
The 
project 
support 
for 
a 
farmer group investment 
subproject 
(FGIS) 
will 
be 
in 
the 
form 
of 
matching 
grants. 
The 
project 
will 
contribute 
50% 
of 
the 
cost 
of 
consumable 
agricultural 
inputs 
(seeds, 
fertilizers 
and 
agro-chemicals) 
up 
to 
approximately 
US$25 
equivalent 
per 
household 
for 
two 
farming 
seasons 
(for 
a 
maximum 
of 
two 
years) 
or 
80% 
of 
costs 
for 
technological innovations 
other 
than 
consumable 
agricultural 
inputs. 
The 
maximum 
amount 
of 
grant 
for FGIS 
per 
village 
will 
be 
US$ 
8,000 
equivalent. 
In 
addition, 
the 
project 
will 
contribute 
100% 
of 
the 
cost 
of 
technical 
assistance 
and 
training 
up 
to 
a 
maximum 
of 
US$ 
3,000. 
Beneficiaries 
will 
be 
required 
to 
contribute 
the 
remaining 
50% 
of 
the 
cost 
of 
consumable 
inputs 
by 
depositing 
cash 
into 
their 
group’s 
bank 
account and 
or 
20% 
of 
the 
cost 
of 
technological innovations. 
The 
total 
cost 
for 
CIS 
for 
the 
five 
year 
period 
is 
estimated at 
US$

1.24 
million. 
(c) 
Strengthening 
Technological 
Linkages 
(US% 
3.76 
million) 
Effective 
linkages 
with 
on-going 
IDA 
and other 
donor 
funded 
projectslprograms 
in 
the 
agricultural 
sector 
will 
be 
established 
for 
complementarity 
purpose. 
The 
project 
will 
achieve 
this 
through 
close 
collaboration 
with 
the 
ASDP 
Secretariat, 
which 
is 
coordinating 
all 
investments 
in 
the 
agricultural 
sector, 
and direct linkages 
to 
on-going 
projects, 
including 
NAEP 
11, 
TAW 
11, 
ASPS, 
SPFS, 
RBMSIIP, PIDP 
and other 
relevant projects implemented 
by 
other 
ministries, 
like 
the 
Agricultural 
Marketing 
Systems 
Development Project 
(AMSDP) 
and 
Tanzania 
Rural 
Financial 
Services 
Project 
(TRFSP). 
Further, 
the 
project 
will 
provide 
support 
for networking 
with 
international 
agricultural 
organizations, 
such 
as 
FAO, 
SG 
2000, 
IITA, 
CIMMYT 
and 
World 
Ago-forestry 
Center, 
in 
order 
to 
continually 
access 
new 
technological options 
for 
dissemination 
to participating 
communities. 
For 
the 
same 
reason, 
PADEP 
will 
support 
regional 
and inter-regional 
study 
exchange 
visits, 
as 
well 
as 
farmer-research-extension-market 
(FREM) 
linkage 
forums. 
During 
PRA 
process, 
the 
project 
will 
provide 
resources 
to 
facilitate 
links 
between 
farmers 
and 
sources 
of 
technological 
options, 
particularly 
at 
the 
stage 
of 
identifying 
technical 
solutions 
to 
the 
identified 
problems. 
Strengthening 
linkages between 
Zonal Agricultural 
Research Centers 
(ZARC) 
and extension 
services, 
which 
are 
provided 
by 
District 
Councils 
will 
ensure 
participation 
of 
beneficiaries 
in 
the 
identification 
of 
ecological-specific 
research 
priorities, 
and 
in 
testing 
the 
options 
available. 
The 
ZARC 
will 
provide 
technical 
services 
to 
the 
local 
authorities 
and 
farming 
communities 
on 
demand-basis. 
District 
Councils 
are 
already 
participating 
in 
identification 
of 
research 
programs 
by 
having 
representatives 
in 
the 
Zonal 
Research 
Internal 
Program 
Reviews 
(IPR), 
Zonal Technical 
Committees 
(ZTC) and 
Zonal 
Executive 
Committees 
(ZEC) 
meetings. 
District 
Councils 
are at 
the 
moment 
contributing 
to 
their 
respective 
Zonal 
Agricultural 
Research 
Funds 
(ZARF). 
In 
turn, 
zonal 
research 
centers 
are 
conducting client-oriented and 
demand-driven adaptive 
research 
to 
solve 
ago-ecological 
specific 
problems. 
District 
capacity 
building 
grants 
could 
be 
used 
to 
contribute 
to 
or 
procure 
services 
from 
zonal 
research centers. 
The project 
will 
finance 
the 
publication 
in 
Kiswahili, 
specific technical 
handbooks 
to 
assist 
sourcing and dissemination 
of 
technological options 
to 
beneficiaries.

Under 
this 
sub-component, 
the 
project 
will 
carry out 
rehabilitation 
and 
equipping 
of 
four 
strategically 
located 
soil 
testing 
laboratories, 
to 
enable 
them 
respond 
efficiently 
to 
the 
growing 
demands 
for 
chemical and 
physical 
analyses 
of 
soils 
from 
communities. 
Knowledge 
of 
soil 
characteristics 
will 
enable 
districts and communities 
choose 
subprojects 
which 
aim 
at 
addressing 
soil 
degradation problems. 
Four 
4-WD 
vehicles 
(one 
for 
each 
laboratory) and 
eight 
motorbikes 
(two 
for 
each 
laboratory) 
will 
be 
provided, 
for 
easy 
access 
to 
remote 
areas 
during 
soil 
surveys 
and 
soil 
sampling. 
This 
will 
also 
facilitate 
monitoring 
and evaluation 
of 
soils 
status 
in 
the 
project 
areas, 
and 
for 
responding 
to 
specific 
demands 
by 
districts, 
communities and 
farmers' 
groups. 
A 
variety 
of 
chemicals and 
laboratory 
equipment, 
including 
20 
soil-testing 
kits 
will 
be acquired 
(5 
per 
laboratory) 
for 
training 
purpose, 
and 
for 
on-site 
soil 
diagnosis. 
Soil 
testing 
kits 
will 
also 
be 
provided 
for 
district 
level 
extension staff. 
Six 
(6) 
kits 
will 
be 
provided 
per district, 
thus 
overall, 
the 
project 
will 
provide 
168 
kits 
for 
28 
participating 
districts. 
The 
costs 
of 
extension 
agents' 
soil 
testing 
kits 
are 
budgeted 
under 
the 
district 
capacity 
building 
grants. 
All 
kits, 
for 
laboratory and districts 
extension 
services, 
will 
be 
procured 
as 
a single 
package. 
It 
is 
estimated 
that 
a 
total 
of 
US$ 
3.76 
equivalent 
million 
will 
be 
spent 
on 
technological linkage activities, 
including 
the 
rehabilitation 
of 
four 
soil 
laboratories. 
Out 
of 
this 
amount 
IDA 
will 
provide 
US$ 
3.43 
million. 
(d) 
The 
project 
will 
facilitate 
coordination 
activities 
at 
community, 
district 
and 
national 
level. 
The 
project 
will 
procure 
nine 
vehicles 
including 
replacements, 
and 
office 
equipment 
for 
use 
by 
the 
Project 
Coordination office. The project 
will 
meet 
operational 
cost 
for 
the 
coordination 
office, 
which 
will 
include 
expenses 
for 
repair and 
maintenance 
of 
vehicles 
and 
office 
equipment, 
staff 
salaries, 
NPSC 
and 
PTC 
meetings, 
facilitation 
and 
coordination 
of 
field 
activities, 
M&E, 
as 
well 
as 
other 
office 
upkeep 
expenses. 
Project Coordination 
and 
Management 
(US% 
4.65 
million) 
PADEP 
will 
adopt 
and 
implement 
a 
Participatory 
Monitoring 
and 
Evaluation 
(PM&E) 
methodology, 
which 
involves 
the 
beneficiaries 
in 
the 
collection, 
processing, storage 
and 
retrieval 
of 
data 
in 
simplified, 
but 
adequate 
formats. 
The project 
will 
support 
the 
design, 
testing 
and 
installation 
of 
a 
computer-based management 
information 
system 
(MIS) 
and 
intensive 
training 
of 
the 
communities, ward, 
district 
and 
national 
level staff 
in 
PM&E 
approaches. 
Baseline 
data 
collection 
will 
be 
undertaken 
as 
part 
of 
an 
extended 
PRA 
exercise 
in 
each 
targeted 
communitylfarmer 
group. 
The 
baseline 
will 
include 
an 
assessment 
of 
the 
quality and availability 
of 
rural 
services 
provision, 
as 
well 
as 
current 
levels 
of 
production, 
asset 
ownership 
and income, 
possibly 
based 
on 
TASAF's Community 
Services 
Delivery 
Survey 
(CSDS) 
methodology. 
Beneficiary-led 
assessments 
will 
be 
carried 
out 
by 
village 
governments 
to 
assess 
the 
benefits 
arising 
from 
subprojects 
as 
they reach 
completion. 
Each district 
will 
summarize 
these 
assessments 
on 
an 
annual 
basis, 
in 
order 
to 
gauge 
the 
emerging 
effects 
of 
subprojects 
on 
the 
communities 
and 
to learn 
lessons 
for 
investments 
in 
following 
years.  These summaries 
will 
then 
be 
reviewed 
at 
annual 
zonal workshops 
attended 
by 
relevant 
stakeholders 
(farmers' 
groups, 
NGOs, 
private 
sector traders, 
etc.). 
The 
M&E 
of 
project 
activities 
will 
also 
involve 
field 
supervision 
by 
PCU 
and 
DFT, 
annual 
project 
evaluations, 
mid-term 
review 
and 
terminal 
evaluation. 
A 
database 
will 
be 
developed 
to 
capture 
all 
the 
data 
and 
information 
generated 
from 
various 
sources. 
To 
enable 
efficient 
processing 
and retrieval, 
computers 
and 
associated accessories 
will 
be 
purchased 
for 
the 
PCU 
and 
participating 
districts, 
depending 
on 
their 
needs 
assessment. 
The 
database 
will 
be 
updated 
regularly to 
accommodate 
new 
requirements 
in 
the 
course 
of 
project 
implementation. 
The 
total 
cost 
for 
project coordination 
activities 
is 
estimated 
at 
US$ 
4.65 
million 
equivalent, 
of 
which 
IDA 
will 
provide 
US$ 
4.30 
million.

Annex 
4 
TANZANIA: Participatory Agricultural Development and 
Empowerment 
Project 
Economic 
and 
Financial 
Analysis 
A 
full 
quantitative evaluation 
of 
economic 
benefits 
of 
the 
project 
cannot 
be 
easily 
done 
for 
several reasons. 
Benefits 
of 
the 
component 
for 
capacity 
building 
and 
institutional 
strengthening, 
which 
includes 
mainly 
training 
and 
studies, are 
difficult 
to quantify 
in 
monetary 
terms. 
The 
investments 
in 
subprojects 
are 
not 
known 
ex 
ante, 
since 
they 
will 
be 
determined 
through 
the 
participatory planning 
processes. 
Benefits 
from 
the subprojects 
focusing 
on 
sustainable 
natural 
resource 
management 
and 
environmental 
conservation 
will 
accrue 
over a 
long 
time 
frame 
and 
in 
part 
derive 
from 
avoidance 
of 
economic loss 
(due 
to 
degradation) 
that 
is 
hard 
to 
measure. 
In 
view 
of 
these 
problems, 
the 
financial 
analysis 
is 
indicative, rather than 
precise. 
Yet 
because 
the 
project 
is 
intended 
to promote 
growth 
of 
incomes, 
financial 
analysis 
is 
needed even 
if 
it 
cannot 
be 
undertaken 
with 
precision. 
The 
costs 
and 
benefits 
of 
adopting 
technologies 
of 
varying intensity 
in 
use 
of 
purchased 
inputs 
in 
Tanzania have 
been 
assessed 
in 
some 
detail. 
The 
costs 
and 
benefits 
of 
the 
actual choices made 
under 
the 
pilot 
projects 
have 
been 
evaluated 
using 
assumptions about 
projected 
benefit 
streams. 
Finally, 
an 
approximate 
sensitivity 
analysis 
has 
been 
undertaken 
to 
suggest 
what 
levels 
of 
income 
growth 
on 
the 
part 
of 
participating 
farmers 
and villages 
would 
be 
needed 
to provide 
a 
positive 
net 
present value 
to 
the 
project. 
The 
following 
sections summarize 
the 
results 
of 
each one 
of 
the 
above-mentioned 
three 
types 
of 
analysis. 
4.1 
The 
Profitability 
of 
Agriculture 
in 
Tanzania 
The financial 
costs 
and 
returns 
for 
seven 
important 
smallholder 
crops 
grown 
in 
five 
areas 
of 
Tanzania 
under 
varying 
degrees 
of 
intensity 
in 
input 
use 
were analyzed 
during 
project 
preparation. 
Overall, 
the 
results 
of 
this 
analysis, based 
on 
current 
production 
trends, 
are 
encouraging and show 
that 
most crops 
offer 
a 
potential 
for 
attractive 
producer 
profits 
and 
good 
returns 
to 
labor. 
The 
data also 
show that a 
range 
of 
technologies 
offer 
higher 
returns, 
and that 
use 
of 
high 
levels 
of 
purchased 
inputs 
is 
rarely 
the 
most 
advantageous approach. 
Especially 
with 
most 
food 
crops, 
that a 
farmer’s 
returns 
can 
improve 
substantially 
compared 
with 
current 
average management 
simply 
through 
the 
use 
of 
organic 
(manure) 
fertilizer 
and 
careful 
adherence 
to 
the fundamentals 
of 
good 
crop 
husbandry 
(timely 
planting 
and 
adequate 
attention 
to 
weed control). 
For 
most 
cash crops, 
on 
the 
other 
hand, 
the 
data 
show that 
the 
use 
of 
purchased 
inputs 
is 
more 
important 
and 
likely 
to 
have a 
positive impact 
on 
profitability. 
The 
data also 
suggest 
that 
a 
rationalization 
of 
regional marketing 
taxes 
as 
well 
as 
an 
investment 
in 
low-cost production 
technologies, 
including 
irrigation 
furrows 
and 
water-based 
hand 
sprayers, 
could 
also 
lead 
to 
substantial 
improvements 
in 
farmer 
income. 
The 
results 
of 
the 
data analysis 
(using 
1997198 
prices 
) 
are 
shown 
below. 
It 
is 
important to 
stress 
that 
the 
numbers 
presented 
here 
should be read 
as 
indicative 
only. 
Farmers 
seeking 
to 
make 
decisions 
about 
investments 
under 
the 
project 
will 
have 
access 
to 
simple decision 
templates 
based 
on 
current 
prices 
and 
costs 
and 
expected 
yields. 
- 
50 
- 
Page  57
Benefits-Cost 
Ratios 
of 
Crop 
Enterprises 
AREA: 
LOCATION: 
Maize 
This 
section 
presents 
the 
quantitative 
results 
for 
each 
of 
the 
seven 
farm 
enterprises 
analyzed. 
To 
provide 
the 
best 
possible 
indication 
of 
the 
costs and 
returns 
to agricultural 
production, 
three 
levels 
of 
smallholder 
management 
are 
analyzed 
for 
each enterprise. 
Specifically, 
the 
first, 
or 
“average” 
production 
level 
is 
characterized 
by 
the 
use 
of 
very 
few 
purchased 
inputs, 
minimal 
attention 
to 
weed 
control 
and 
fairly 
poor 
yield 
results. 
The 
second, 
or 
“improved” 
level 
is 
based 
on 
better 
management, 
including 
good 
weed 
control, 
the 
use 
of 
manure 
fertilizer, 
slightly more 
intensive 
input 
use 
and 
higher 
yield 
results. 
This 
is 
a 
level 
of 
increased investment 
in 
inputs 
that 
could 
be 
financed 
under 
the 
small 
outlays 
envisaged 
for 
the 
farmer groups 
under 
the 
PADEP 
project. 
The 
third, 
or 
“potential” 
level 
assumes 
good 
weed 
control 
and 
satisfactory 
use 
of 
pesticides, 
inorganic fertilizers 
and 
other 
recommended 
inputs. 
Importantly, 
as 
used 
here, 
the 
term 
“potential” 
does 
not 
refer 
to 
yields 
attainable 
under 
ideal 
conditions 
on 
a 
research 
station, 
but 
only to 
the 
output 
a 
very 
good 
smallholder 
can expect 
in 
a 
year 
with 
adequate 
rainfall. 
A 
complete 
list 
of 
all 
crops 
and 
locations analyzed 
is 
given 
in 
Table 
1.

A. 
Maize 
Maize 
is 
Tanzania’s 
principal food 
crop 
and 
is 
grown 
in 
almost 
every 
region 
of 
the 
country. 
Although 
other foods 
(including 
cassava 
and 
banana) 
are 
of 
greater 
importance 
in 
specific 
locations, 
most 
farmers 
still 
cultivate 
maize 
to 
some 
extent 
or 
another 
to 
meet 
at 
least 
some 
of 
their 
subsistence 
needs. 
At 
the 
national 
level, 
this 
can 
be 
seen 
in 
the 
fact that 
maize 
production 
has 
more 
or 
less 
kept 
pace 
with 
population 
growth 
for 
the 
past years. 
In 
some 
areas, 
maize 
is 
also 
grown 
as 
the 
main 
cash 
crop. 
This 
is 
especially 
true 
in 
parts 
of 
the 
Southern 
Highlands 
and 
near 
Arusha 
in 
the 
north 
where 
growing 
conditions 
are 
well 
suited 
to 
this 
activity. 
Cash 
sales 
are 
also 
common 
in 
border regions 
where farmers 
selling 
to 
export 
traders are sometimes 
paid 
significantly more 
than 
those 
selling 
on 
the 
local 
market. 
For 
most smallholder 
farmers 
with 
limited 
sources 
of 
income, 
there 
is 
pressure 
each year 
to 
sell 
at 
least 
some 
maize 
for 
cash 
early 
in 
the 
marketing 
season 
when prices 
are 
low. 
In 
these 
cases, 
farmers 
-51 
- 
Page  58
are 
often 
forced 
to 
buy 
back 
maize (or 
other 
food) 
later 
in 
the 
year 
for 
home consumption 
at a 
much 
higher 
price. 
At 
least 
on 
the 
traded 
portion 
of 
their 
crop, therefore, 
many 
households 
appear 
to 
be 
making 
a 
financial 
loss 
from 
the 
cultivation 
of 
maize. 
Although 
the 
production 
of 
food 
crops 
for 
home 
consumption 
makes 
good 
sense 
for 
other 
reasons, 
this 
pattern certainly 
does 
nothing to 
alleviate 
the 
high 
levels 
of 
rural 
poverty 
and 
it 
is 
important 
to look for 
ways 
of 
breaking 
this 
cycle. 
The 
quantitative 
analysis 
of 
maize 
was 
carried out 
for five 
locations. 
Although Mbeya 
is 
the 
only 
location 
typically 
regarded 
as 
a 
major 
maize 
producing 
area, 
the 
costs 
and returns 
will 
not 
be 
too 
different 
from 
other 
surplus 
areas 
also 
in 
the Southern 
Highlands 
with 
a 
similar 
climate. 
Areas 
where 
maize 
is 
grown 
mostly 
as 
a 
secondary 
crop 
were 
included to provide 
an 
idea 
of 
possible 
food 
security 
strategies 
for 
farmers 
in 
these 
locations. 
Because 
maize, 
sorghum and 
rice 
are 
grown 
both for 
cash 
sales 
and 
subsistence, 
farmer 
profits 
are 
calculated 
for 
growers that 
sell 
different 
shares 
of 
total 
output. 
In 
these 
cases, 
food 
stocks 
retained 
for 
home 
consumption 
are 
valued 
at 
the 
higher 
imputed 
cost 
of 
purchasing 
grain 
eight months 
after 
harvest 
when 
local 
supplies 
are 
scarce. 
Improved 
production 
assumes 
the 
use 
of 
manure 
fertilizer; 
potential 
management 
is 
based 
on 
four 
50 
Kg 
bags 
of 
fertilizer 
per 
hectare. 
Table 
2 
looks 
at 
the 
benefits 
of 
technological 
advancement 
as 
measured 
by 
incremental 
cost-benefit 
ratios 
(ICBRs). 
It 
is 
generally 
assumed 
that where 
the 
ICBR 
is 
greater 
than 
2, 
the 
risk 
of 
investing 
additional 
resources can 
be 
justified; 
when 
the 
ICBR 
is 
less 
than 
2, 
farmers 
are 
likely 
to 
be 
better-off producing 
at the 
lower 
input 
level.

show that 
the

are 
far 
better 
for

who 
adopt 
improved 
technology than 
for 
those 
who 
go 
on 
to 
use 
inorganic 
fertilizers 
at a 
higher 
level 
of 
intensity. 
Even 
when 
measured 
against 
the 
crop’s 
higher 
imputed 
food 
security 
value, 
the 
incremental 
returns 
from 
investing 
in 
fertilizer 
at 
high 
rates 
of 
use are 
still 
poor.

B. 
Sorghum 
Sorghum 
is 
grown mainly 
in 
areas 
with 
a 
harsh, 
dry 
climate 
where 
it 
is 
an 
important 
staple 
food. 
The 
ICBRs for 
sorghum 
shown 
in 
Table 
3 
indicate 
that 
the 
use 
of 
inorganic fertilizers and 
other 
purchased 
inputs 
at 
the 
potential 
level 
is 
difficult 
to 
justify 
compared 
to 
the 
returns 
from 
manure 
fertilizer 
and 
other 
simple 
technologies.

C. 
Rice 
Compared 
with 
maize 
and 
sorghum, 
most 
rice 
farmers 
sell 
a 
greater 
share 
of 
their 
total 
output. 
Although rice 
is 
still 
important 
for 
subsistence, 
there 
is 
good 
market 
demand 
for 
this 
commodity 
and 
most 
farmers 
aim 
to 
sell 
at 
least 
some 
of 
their production 
each year 
for 
cash. 
Broad 
production 
figures 
indicate 
this 
pattern 
has 
become 
increasingly common 
in 
that 
total 
rice 
production 
has 
been 
growing 
faster 
than 
the 
increase 
in 
national 
population. 
One advantage 
of 
Tanzanian 
rice 
is 
that 
most 
farmers 
grow 
a 
large 
grain, 
aromatic 
variety 
regarded 
as 
being 
of 
very 
high 
quality.  This 
offers 
a 
good 
opportunity 
for 
niche 
marketing 
and 
to 
attract 
premium 
prices 
for 
export.  Tanzania 
does 
export 
some 
rice, 
but 
is 
currently 
a 
net 
importer 
of 
this 
commodity. 
Table 
4 
looks 
at 
the 
incremental benefits 
of 
adopting 
improved 
and 
potential 
management. 
For 
rain fed 
rice, 
these 
data 
show that a 
move 
from 
average 
to improved 
management 
provides 
a better rate 
or 
return 
than a 
move 
from 
improved 
to 
potential. 
In 
the 
case 
of 
irrigated 
rice, the 
opposite 
is 
true 
and 
the 
returns 
from 
producing 
at 
the 
potential 
level 
are 
much 
more 
attractive 
than 
from 
advancing 
to 
the 
improved 
level 
only.

D. 
Cotton 
A 
widely 
held 
perception 
is 
that 
the 
cotton 
sector 
is 
in 
decline. 
This 
position 
is 
not 
necessarily 
supported 
by 
broad production 
figures 
which 
show great 
variation 
in 
output 
from 
year 
to 
year, 
but 
do 
not 
indicate 
any 
clearly 
discernible 
trend. 
It 
is, 
however, clear that 
the 
cotton 
sector 
suffers 
from 
many 
problems. 
Chief 
among 
these 
is 
the 
poor quality 
of 
seed 
planted 
by 
most farmers. 
Specifically, 
most 
of 
the 
seed 
currently 
used 
in 
Tanzania 
has 
been 
recycled 
for 
more 
than 
20 
years. 
This 
results 
in 
extremely 
poor 
yields, 
even 
by 
African 
standards, 
of 
less 
than 
300 
Kglha 
on 
many 
farms.  These 
outdated 
seeds 
also 
provide 
a 
very 
low 
ginning 
outtum 
(GOT) 
of 
only 
about 33% 
lint. 
By 
comparison, 
smallholder cotton 
in 
Zambia 
yields 
about 
39% 
lint; 
in 
Zimbabwe, 
42% 
is 
the 
norm. 
Because 
lint 
is 
the 
most 
valuable 
product 
of 
cotton 
(the 
other 
being 
seed), 
this 
low 
GOT 
prevents 
more 
attractive prices 
from 
being 
offered to 
the 
farmer. 
Clearly, 
efforts 
to 
provide 
farmers 
with 
improved 
planting 
material 
are 
likely 
to 
generate 
substantial 
benefits. 
This 
challenge 
is 
already 
being 
addressed 
by 
the 
cotton 
industry. 
Research 
programs 
are 
underway 
and 
from 
next 
year 
it 
is 
expected 
that 
a 
multiplication 
program 
can 
begin 
so 
that clean 
(not 
fuzzy) 
seed 
will 
be 
available 
for 
sale 
to 
farmers 
by 
the 
2002/03 
growing 
season.

Mbeya 
(Southern 
I-Eghlands) 
- 
Ec 
In 
terms 
of 
profitability, 
these 
data 
clearly 
show 
that 
cotton 
becomes 
significantly 
more 
profitable 
at 
each successive management 
level. 
This 
is 
especially 
true 
for 
farmers 
who 
carefully 
follow 
all 
crop 
recommendations 
and produce 
at the 
potential 
level 
where 
profits 
are 
substantially 
greater 
than 
with 
average 
or 
improved 
management. 
Although 
the 
estimated 
net 
profits 
from 
cotton 
are 
lower 
than 
for 
all 
other 
cash crops analyzed, 
cotton 
is 
well 
suited 
to 
relatively 
harsh 
growing 
conditions. 
For 
farmers 
in 
these 
areas, 
cotton 
offers 
one 
of 
the 
best 
opportunities 
for 
a 
cash 
income. 
E. 
Tobacco 
Both 
flue 
and 
dark-fire 
cured 
tobaccos 
are 
grown 
in 
Tanzania.  These 
varieties 
must 
be 
heat 
cured 
using 
firewood 
in 
specially 
constructed 
barns 
before 
the 
crop 
can 
be 
sold. 
Air-cured, 
burley 
tobacco 
is 
not 
common 
due 
to 
large variations 
in 
humidity 
during 
the 
curing 
season. 
Since 
the 
start 
of 
the 
decade, 
total 
tobacco 
production 
has 
increased 
from 
only 
about 
12,000 
mt 
in 
1990191 
to 
more than 
35,000 
mt 
in 
the 
most recent 
growing 
season. 
Of 
this 
production, flue-cured 
tobacco 
is 
by 
far 
the 
most 
important and 
regularly 
accounts 
for 
about 
80% 
of 
the 
total. 
Fire-cured 
tobacco 
is 
only 
common 
in 
parts 
of 
Ruvuma Region 
in 
the 
south; 
flue-cured 
tobacco 
is 
grown 
in 
many 
locations throughout 
the 
country.

- 
Page  61

These 
data 
show 
that 
tobacco 
production 
can 
be 
extremely 
profitable 
where 
both 
flue 
and 
fire-cured 
tobacco 
provide significantly more 
income 
per 
hectare 
compared 
with 
all 
other 
enterprises 
analyzed. 
For 
the 
majority 
of 
farmers 
that 
only 
cultivate 
one acre 
of 
tobacco, 
however, the 
returns 
will 
obviously 
be far more 
modest. 
A 
typical 
farmer 
growing 
one acre 
of 
flue-cured 
tobacco 
with 
average 
management 
in 
Mbeya, 
for 
example, 
can 
expect 
a 
cash 
income 
of 
about 
TShs 
212,880. 
This 
is 
still 
very 
good, 
but 
is 
only 
comparable 
to 
the 
income 
that 
could 
be 
earned 
from 
a 
full 
hectare 
of 
most 
other 
(easier 
to 
manage) crops. 
F. 
Coffee 
Coffee production 
has 
been 
on 
a 
slow 
decline 
for 
at 
least 
the 
past 
20 
years. 
This 
is 
largely 
explained 
by 
fact that 
most 
coffee 
trees 
in 
Tanzania 
are 
now 
more 
than 
70 
years 
old 
and 
so 
produce 
very 
little 
yield. 
Because these 
very 
old 
trees 
do 
not 
respond 
well 
to 
inputs, 
a 
major 
challenge 
is 
to 
replant 
with 
newer 
varieties. 
The 
quality 
of 
Tanzanian 
coffee 
is 
also 
thought 
to 
have deteriorated 
in 
recent 
years. 
Partly, this 
is 
because 
private 
buyers 
do 
not 
make 
any 
distinction 
in 
quality when 
buying 
at 
the 
farmgate 
so 
there 
has 
been 
little 
incentive 
for 
farmers 
to 
use 
good 
management. 
Likewise, 
very 
intense 
competition 
between buyers 
means 
that 
most 
are 
willing 
to 
accept 
whatever 
product 
they 
can 
get 
regardless 
of 
quality. 
Quantitative 
results 
from 
the 
analysis 
of 
coffee 
are 
summarized 
below.  Annualized 
investment 
costs 
at 
the 
improved 
and

Taken 
together, 
these 
results 
are 
very good and 
show that 
coffee offers an 
opportunity 
for 
highly 
attractive 
producer 
profits, 
especially 
with 
improved 
and 
potential 
management 
where 
the 
returns 
are 
much 
greater 
than 
with 
current 
average 
management. 
Even 
at 
the 
low 
input 
level, 
however, coffee 
still 
provides 
a 
very good 
net 
income 
from 
zero 
expenditure 
on 
cash 
inputs. 
This 
is 
a 
significant 
advantage 
of 
coffee and 
means 
that 
even 
very 
poor 
households 
are 
likely 
to 
benefit 
from 
this 
crop 
in 
areas 
with 
a 
suitable 
climate. 
Likewise, 
for 
improved 
farmers, 
the 
cash 
requirement 
is 
still 
relatively 
modest 
so 
that 
technological 
advancement 
may 
be 
easier 
to 
achieve 
than 
with 
many 
other 
crops. 
In 
all 
cases, 
the 
ICBRs 
and other 
rates 
of 
retum 
are 
excellent and 
show that 
an 
investment 
of 
cash 
and 
other 
resources 
in 
coffee 
makes 
good financial 
sense. 
In 
terms 
of 
labor 
requirements, 
the 
data show 
that 
coffee 
is 
very 
labor 
intensive, 
but 
still 
provides 
an 
excellent 
retum 
per day worked. 
Importantly, 
most 
of 
the 
labor 
invested 
in 
coffee 
is 
spent 
on 
pulping 
and 
processing 
which 
is 
done 
late 
in 
the 
season 
when 
there 
are 
fewer 
demands 
on 
a farmer's 
time. 
With 
the 
continued 
introduction 
of 
pulping 
by 
private 
buyers, 
the 
total 
labor requirement 
for 
coffee 
could 
be 
substantially 
reduced. 
G. 
Cashew 
Cashew 
production 
has 
increased 
dramatically 
since 
the 
introduction 
of 
market 
reforms. 
This 
is 
mainly 
a 
result 
of 
the 
higher producer prices 
now 
being 
offered 
by 
private 
buyers 
(about 
60-65% 
of 
the 
fob 
price) 
compared 
with 
the former 
Cashew 
Marketing Board 
(about 
40% 
of 
fob). 
Also, 
farmers 
are 
now 
paid 
in 
cash 
at 
the 
time 
of 
sale 
whereas 
the 
CMB 
often 
paid 
several 
months 
late.  These 
developments 
both 
provide good 
incentive 
for 
farmers 
to 
adopt 
better 
management practices, 
including 
time 
spent 
slashing 
grasses 
and constructing 
fire 
breaks. 
This 
is 
in 
stark contrast 
with 
the 
period 
before 
liberalization 
when 
many 
farmers 
left 
their 
trees 
untended 
and 
often 
did 
not 
bother 
to 
harvest 
the 
nuts 
at 
all. 
Cashews 
are 
grown 
mainly 
in 
coastal regions. 
Nuts 
grown 
in 
the 
south 
yield 
a 
higher 
outturn when 
cracked 
and 
are 
widely 
regarded 
as 
being 
superior 
in 
quality 
to 
those 
from 
the 
north.  Last 
year, 
for 
example, 
nuts 
from 
the 
southem 
cashew area 
attracted 
an 
average 
price 
of 
US$9OO/mt 
fob 
Mtwara. 
Cashew 
grown 
in 
the 
northem 
producing 
area, 
sold 
for 
about 
US$750/MT 
fob 
Tanga. 
Results 
from 
the 
quantitative 
analysis 
are 
presented 
in 
Table 
8.

of 
pesticide.

The 
Whole 
Project 
Break-Even 
Rate 
of 
Return 
A

was

to

of

of

project. 
The 
model 
indicates that

investment 
under

cannot

1.8%

growth 
rate 
per 
year, 
which 
corresponds 
to 
an 
internal 
rate 
of 
return to 
the 
PADEP 
investment (economic 
and financial) 
of 
at 
least 
12%. 
The 
results 
suggest 
in 
other 
words 
that 
the 
PADEP 
investment 
can 
be 
justified 
if 
it 
leads 
to 
an 
increase 
of 
11 
percent 
or 
more 
in 
the 
real

rural 
communities 
in 
about 
one

(nine

of 
project 
implementation.
